		<date>2005-05-13</date>
		<username>Kueko</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>"Stepford" as robotic as its wives</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Stepford Wives", in a similar fashion to the film "Pleasantville", sets out to be a satirical comedy, lampooning the ideals of the 1950s--specifically, the perfect homemaker wife. Unfortunately, quite unlike "Pleasantville", "The Stepford Wives" fails miserably.Contrary to the claims made by the posters and trailers for the film, the plot of "The Stepford Wives" is no secret. No thanks to the trailers which reveal many of the films secrets, most moviegoers will already be familiar with the mystery of Stepford.That said, the story revolves around Joanna, a highly ambitious and incredibly successful television executive. Her career comes to an abrupt end when one of the reality shows from her new fall lineup causes disaster. In an attempt to save his wife's sanity, Joanna's husband, Walter moves her and their kids to the gated-community of Stepford ? a town with "no crime, no poverty, and no pushing." But, of course, the reason Stepford and its women are so perfect lies in the fact that they are robots ... or are they? One of the main problems the audience must face while watching "The Stepford Wives" is the enormous plot holes, especially in the case of the wives, who seem to change from robots to normal women with brain alterations and back again whenever the plot calls for it. Or rather, to whichever is funnier at the given moment. The issue is, robots or not, it's NOT funny, and most certainly not clever, as a satire should be.The film shows promise in the first act, as we are first introduced to the bizarre antics of the wives (partaking in homemaker aerobics, "Let's be washing-machines!" and discussing the "controversial" Christmas crafts book during their book club sessions). As we reach the second act however, the film shifts gears into breakneck speed, (with each sequence lasting no more than five minutes) as we watch Joanna slowly solve the mystery of Stepford. This might have been more engaging had the audience not already been aware of the secret long before Joanna.As "The Stepford Wives" crawls into the ending stretch, it backpedals, rapidly unraveling with one twist piled onto another in an attempt to keep the audience guessing. Sadly, it only confuses us more, until, finally, the plot is so convoluted we can no longer tell what's going on.The movie constantly contradicts itself, right until the bitter end. Some say to enjoy a comedy, you must suspend disbelief--I agree, but in the case of "The Stepford Wives", the comedy is almost non-existent. The film had so much potential, but inevitably, fell short.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Alan Farris</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Competently made, if a little self-conscious and dated</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Firstly, let me just make the following points: I have not seen the original movie (or, if I have, I do not remember it), but I do know the premise of the story, as a classic science fiction thriller. Furthermore, I did not go to the cinema intending to see it, but I ended up seeing it anyway.The makers of the movie seem very conscious of the fact that they are producing a re-make; I think that weakens it, as you cannot run forwards whilst continuously glancing over your shoulder.Glenn Close demonstrates, once again, what a marvelous character actress she is, although as she became more and more hysterical I began to expect her stuff a bunny in a pan of boiling water. Christopher Walken barely gets out of first gear - his acting is (appropriately!) robotic. Matthew Broderick is underused; for someone who was so good as Ferris Bueller I think that he could have been more effectively employed and his character fleshed out.There is some confusion (see the noticeboard) about whether the wives were real or robots. This arises from the original movie, in which the wives were replaced by synthetic versions of themselves (i.e. robots), which pleased their husbands as they were far more compliant, co-operative and harder-working than their live predecessors. In this version the wives (who are star women of industry etc) brains have silicon chips implanted in them that make them electronically controllable (by the husbands). So their bodies are as before, but their brains have been modified. Th exception to this is the Christopher Walken character, who really is a man-made (or actually, the Glenn Close character-made) robot!The moral dilemma of the movie is which is better - the clean, tidy, beautiful nirvana of the electronically controlled Stepford world or the grungy, disorganised real world. Glenn's character prefers the former, Nicole Kidman's character spends the film vacillating (look it up!) between the former and the latter. It comes down to a re-assessment of the idea of beauty, which, in the days of Charlie's Angels and leggy blonde's of the 1970's, was more of an adventurous concept to debate by means of film than it is almost 30 years later. Bette Midler does her best to demonstrate the two extremes; her "real world" house is a health and safety death trap and she dresses as if dragged through a hedge backwards, but when she is transformed her "Barbie Doll" version is a scary mimic of Glenn Close's.The ironic twist of this all is that Nicole Kidman looks incredibly sexy with short black hair at her very lowest point and similarly gorgeous as a brunette before her "transformation" into a much less attractive plastic blonde, which either undermines the argument of the film or justifies her stance, depending upon your point of view.Whilst no-one comes out of this film looking bad, the reputations of the main characters, with possibly Nicole excepted, will hardly have been enhanced by a notion that may have seemed plausible thirty years ago, but nowadays looks a little quaint.Alan F</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2004-06-18</date>
		<username>unbend_5440</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Another 70s Sci-Fi remake mistake</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Take two things into account with this review.#1- The Stepford Wives movie from 1975 is one of my favourite movies.#2- I completely understood that this remake was going to be something different.In fact, I was so accepting of the fact that Frank Oz chose to make this a satirical comedy, that I was actually looking forward to the many changes and updates that this remake was going to have. Even walking out of the theatre, I was judging this very much as it's own movie. Having said that, I have to say that I don't completely dislike this movie, I just felt very let down, not just as a fan of The Stepford Wives, but as a normal filmgoer as well. There are a lot of good things offered in this remake. The cast is outstanding. I disagree with many who think Matthew Broderick was underused. I thought he was perfectly used, and I liked that they made his character more sympathetic. In a way, you have more respect for him than you do Joanna, unlike the original. And also unlike the original, Joanna is very sleezy, and overall a horrible human being with some redeemable qualities. Being a straight male, I almost feel weird saying this, but Bette Midler was perhaps the best thing about the movie. She put an interesting spin on the character of Bobbie. Most of the jokes are clever, and for the most part classy.But here is the major problem. Frank Oz made the horrible mistake of updating this movie and changing a lot of the story, just for the sake of changing it. I have a feeling that he wanted to do something different to make this his own movie, but never thought about it hard enough to make it different in a clever way. The climax plays out just like the original, and has the supermarket scene with the Stepford Wives, but then there's a whole "second ending" that not only seems like a sloppy and thoughtless twist, but it makes very little sense. This is the same mistake that Planet Of The Apes and Rollerball both made with their remakes. They tried to surprise the audience with a new shocking twist, but it's not thought out or well executed, and it's just plain dumb. Then my other big complaint is the pacing of the movie. There's far too much "filler" in the middle section. Scenes that draw out the inevitable un-necessarily. Then when it finally comes time for the real excitement in the climax, they rush through it. I was very pleased how Oz filmed and pieced together the climax. Even the way the confrontation at the mansion was changed up, I was impressed. But what was the sense of condensing the best part of the movie into a few rushed minutes when there were several scenes in the middle section that were tediously dull? Ultimately, it's the various new twists and cheap modern day updates that bury this movie. It's not at all that I objected to the changes as a fan of the original, it's because they don't fit the basic theme and tone of the rest of the movie. So many of these modern day changes (just for the sake of changing) feel wildly out of place.There is a lot of good material and great scenes, and for the most part the story is told well. I think when this comes out on video and DVD I'm going to put this on tape, minus some of the scenes from the middle, and minus the second ending. I'm hoping I'll enjoy it a lot more that way. One more thing. Why did they change the knife scene????? A hand on a stove element? Lame substitution.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>I am a major fan of the original Stepford wives and Ira Levin's book. I rank the original among my favorite movies ever and have been waiting for this movie for at least a year. And I saw it opening night. And loved it.This seems to be a love it or hate it type of movie and I can see why in a way. My thoughts on the movie-SPOILERS!If anyone comes in expecting to see an exact replica(LOL) of the original they will be possibly let down, the basic concept and characters, names are the same but this is Stepford in today's modern society. And it is not a horror it is a dark comedy. More comedy then dark. Funny-almost TO funny-wickedly camp-a few times a bit creepy-and features an amazing cast. But know what your watching before your watching it,this isn't an exact remake although there will be certain familiar elements.But this is not stepford wives as we knew it all those years ago-oh the lovely ladies of stepford are still there as mechanical as ever, as they do their exercise steps so flawlessly, by pretending to be washing machines. But this movie has been done as a comedy which I went in prepared for as I had heard about the movie and been on the IMDB stepford wives board so I knew(a little) what I was getting into. I imagine however for those that want to see the whole first movie done again they will be disapointed-or maybe not-if one can say- (hey, it's a comedy-stepford wives(the original) spoofs the husbands that want these perfect women and now the modern day stepford wives spoofs the whole original stepford wives) then maybe one can have a good time with this. And what a time it will be! I haven't laughed that hard at a movie theater in a LONG time-and I see many, many movies-at several points I thought I was gonna have to leave the theater. In fact, judging from the reaction of pretty much the majority of people in there, including my friends, many felt the same. The whole theater was laughing so hard it was impossible to get and hear everything and when the movie was over everyone broke into claps. I haven't seen THAT in awhile-not even at Shrek2 which I saw the week before. Or maybe it's just that that we are from Connecticut....(Great home state tribute by the way!)We broke into clapping at that to....The cast was fantastic. Nicole Kidman shows she can pretty much do any type of movie and do it well-Glen Close and Christopher Walken were great-Mathew Broderick brought a certain something to Walter Eberhart-a much nicer guy then in the original-Faith Hill-to underused-her part should have been bigger but she was still good and at leat she got to spark! Colorful role though her screen time was much to limited. And how about the new plot line of a gay couple-Quite good, I enjoyed the whole cast.But the best role in the film, without question, had to belong to Bette Midler. She absolutely made the movie, she had the best lines(pine cones anyone?), best scenes, and even though the movie was completely different from the original she still made Bobbie recognizable-a perfect casting fit.My ONLY complaint(alas-heard quite frequently already) is the ending-the last five minutes I could have lived without. The final "twist" involving sparking mens' heads, Glen close suddenly morphing into Alex from Fatal Attraction , and a twist that puts the whole previous premise of the "robots" into complete senselessness, was not in keeping with the rest of the movie and quite frankly it was bewildering why that was even thrown in, in the first place.The movie was strong enough. It didn't need it and detracted from it. (Hey, there's camp and there's complete absurdity.)This movie however, for all it's controversy, mixed reviews and strange baffling final minutes, is hysterical. And that's quite a compliment. It is very difficult to 1) MAKE a funny movie at all whether it's a remake or completely original and: 2) remake any film especially one that really became a household name like stepford wives. That the movie pulls it off says alot about the talent and creativity that went into making it. Being from the "home of the city of stepford", Connecticut, alot of the fun as well, was seeing my beautiful state(we don't get THAT many movies filmed here) and seeing an accomplished group of talented, well respected actors/actresses camp it up in a fine homage to one of my favorite movies of all time. Even if you've never seen the original(as had not the people in front of us) please-see it for the entertainment factor. You'll laugh so hard you might spit out the movie popcorn.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-06-11</date>
		<username>johnnysugar</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Welcome to Stepford, population: Camp, POTENTIAL SPOILERS HEREIN!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>WARNING! POSSIBLE SPOILERS HEREIN!In 1975, Ira Levin's book "The Stepford Wives" was made into a film. The movie, like the book, was a horror/science-fiction treatise on the women's movement about an idyllic little town where the men turned their feminist wives into subservient June Cleaver clones. In retrospect, the movie was both chilling in its premise and amazingly campy in its execution, like much of Levin's work ("Rosemary's Baby", "Sliver"). The new adaptation of Levin's book (since it's not exactly a remake of the 1975 film) decides to jettison the horror aspect and go straight for the camp factor. In this it succeeds, but the film is wildly uneven at times and often feels as if it has itself been a victim of the Stepford machinery.Joanna Eberhart (Nicole Kidman) is the powerful and respected head of the EBS television network. When a disgruntled contestant on one of her reality shows goes on a shooting spree, Joanna is fired to avoid damaging lawsuits. Her husband Walter (Matthew Broderick) decides that the family needs a new start and moves to the small gated community of Stepford, Connecticut. Soon Joanna finds that all of the women in Stepford are smiling, well-groomed, and live only to serve their husbands, who spend all of THEIR time at the Stepford Men's Association. The Stepford women even wear their best dresses to aerobics class, where all their exercises mimic household chores, and to the book club, where they discuss books on decorating. The only others who find the situation bizarre are Bobbie Markowitz (Bette Midler) and Roger Bannister (Roger Bart), and soon the three are trying to get to the bottom of Stepford's secrets.As might be expected, the best performances of the film come from the Stepford wives themselves. Kidman is always a pleasure to watch on screen, and Midler has some of the best one-liners in the film even though she's playing a character she's played a dozen times over. Bart steals nearly every scene he's in as the only Stepford wife who's a gay man (and quite the little flamer to boot). Glenn Close is hilarious as Claire Wellington, the town's matron, and Faith Hill has some very amusing moments in her brief screen time. The men, however, don't come off nearly as well. Broderick is woefully miscast, and he never seems to bring any spark to his milquetoast of a husband. Christopher Walken, as the leader of the Men's Association, doesn't even appear to be trying, and his appearance in a supposed-to-be-retro-campy commercial for Stepford is painful.Director Frank Oz and screenwriter Paul Rudnick have both done fine work in their careers, and they make a valiant effort to film a dark satirical comedy, but they're just not quite up to the task. Oz's direction is quite static, and there are times in the film when it's clear that the cast wasn't being cooperative (rumors of on-set fighting ran rampant during production). Rudnick is a master of campy, zingy one-liners, and while the movie is filled with several genuinely funny moments and lines, there is little connecting them. It's as if he was so concerned with filling the script with jokes that he forgot a plot or character development. Characters come and go too quickly for us to become invested in them, and Kidman and Broderick have no chemistry to speak of. There are also several inconsistencies with how the wives are presented, which are never resolved or explained, although the movie's brisk pace and re-shot ending suggest that such details may have existed at one time. Additionally, the film tends to alternate between campy farce and techno-thriller, as if Oz and Rudnick were never sure of which end to play.Despite all these drawbacks, the film is still watchable and entertaining with more than one moment of drop-dead humor. Enough moments, in fact, that it almost makes up for the disappointingly predictable ending. In addition, the opening credit sequence is brilliant, a montage of near-fetishistic scenes of 1950's housewives dancing in absurdly futuristic kitchens. Worth a trip to the theatre or video store, for sure, but make sure you've got a high tolerance for camp. 6 out of 10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Better than the Original 1970s Movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I am not a fan of the original movie. When I first heard that there was going to be a re-make, I thought it was a bloody stupid idea. Lots, and I mean LOTS, of broad laughs beginning straight away with the clever tongue-in-cheek opening credits. Excellent production values.The only thing I didn't like was a messy climax that wasn't consistent with the rest of the movie, both in tone and logic. I understand that in today's post-modern world, the studio had to tweak the ending but it felt like they pulled the ending out of their ass because they were stuck on what to do. .. which is probably the case.So there you have it: an enjoyable, funny, mostly mindless 90 minutes. Certainly no Disaster as rumors would lead you to believe.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Forget the Original: This is a comedy, guys</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Some critics disparage this film as a flimsy remake of the 1975 original but: Though the premise is the same, the Frank Oz movie is a comedy from start until the credits roll. I don't think of Nicole Kidman as a comedienne (nor is she much of an actress) but she handles this part very well. She exaggerates appropriately as the TV exec fired because of a "reality" project based on sex that threatens to ruin her network and subsequently as the post-nervous breakdown wife moved to Stepford to recover from the wreckage of life as a superstar. Moreover, while I doubt many people will agree with me, Kidman is far more attractive as a brunette than as a blonde. She reverts to her usual blonde self only at the end of the film when she's presented as a blonde robot. I wonder if she understood the irony. In short, Kidman is okay, though the comedy is supplied mostly by Matthew Broderick as her husband and by Bette Midler, Glenn Close, Christopher Walken (parodying his usual persona more or less) and Roger Bart and David Marshall Grant as a gay couple also newly arrived in Stepford. If seen without preconceptions based on the earlier movie, the new edition of Stepford Wives can be quite entertaining. I give it an 8.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Watch the original first, then enjoy this version...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Just viewed the DVD of the 2004 version and absolutely loved it. I so disagree with most reviewers here. *SPOILERS AHEAD* The new version satirizes the original, is meant to be pure comedy with a little bit of drama and suspense thrown in. This new version is an "inside joke" that you will only find amusing if you've seen the first. Sure, we're supposed to consider men's roles vs. women's roles in our society, but that is simply a by-product from spoofing a movie where gender roles were the heavy-handed focal point. If you haven't seen the Kidman WIVES yet, do yourself a favor and watch the Ross WIVES first. For example, would The Brady Bunch Movie have been as enjoyable if one had never seen an episode of the TV series? I personally don't think so. Think of this new version as a SATIRE of the FIRST, NOT a REMAKE. Then you can enjoy, as I did, the fact that the husbands are such nerds, the wives were such powerhouses before their transformations, the happy ending, the over-the-top performances which are just hysterical. You can ignore the inconsistencies and holes. The whole movie is implausible after all, so 'so what' if the Wives are suddenly transformed back with a reversal of the messages to the chips in their brains, whereas Christopher Walken was irreversibly transformed? But it doesn't really matter. The journey is the fun part of this movie, not the destination. Another movie that this reminds me of is "Mars Attacks." Satire is meant to be fun, not taken seriously. CHILL OUT FOLKS - ENJOY THE MOVIE!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>khaley-1</username>
		<score>0.1</score>
		<shortDescription>Guaranteed to repulse anyone with taste and dignity</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Five times during its neverending 1.5 hours I considered walking out of this movie; but I was with four other people in the theatre, so I decided against possibly insulting them or ruining the movie for them--just in case the film hadn't already insulted their taste or ruined itself for them. For someone who usually finds some redeeming qualities in every film experience, and who is interested in all genres and extremely open-minded, this vehement speech I give against The Stepford Wives is meant as a general warning to all people not to waste their 1.5 hours like myself--to spare their dignity and taste, and avoid their being haunted later by some of the vulgar, repulsive images and ideas put forth by the film. The modes of comedy, horror, thriller, drama, and science fiction alternate in unpredictable and unpleasant fits and starts throughout, with excruciating non-transitions linking them. The dialogue is as shoddy and trite as dialogue can be; the score sounds like tawdry elevator music and consistently tries to drown out the poor dialogue (unfortunately, without complete success); the tone with which the material is handled is disastrously ill-chosen--when striking a feminist note the film comes across as pathetic and sentimental; when striking a historically particular chauvinist note the film is dull and offensive in its baseball-bat-over-the-audience's-head polemic; when attempting to strike a tone of technological innovation and sci-fi imagination, the film flops for its utterly improbable, illogical, simplistic, inelegant presentation of devices, practices, and themes (the scene where Broderick shuts down all the computer clones comes readily to mind--are the women controlled by a central device, or are they clones, or are they the same women, slightly reprogrammed, with new bodies, or....? the film cannot decide and it's painfully obvious to any child who's lived this life in our world for six years at least that none of this is either possible, believable, or even pleasant to conceive). I dare not think that the two very intelligent main actors--Nicole Kidman and Matthew Broderick--could be anything less than deeply ashamed when they hear the name of this movie mentioned.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Nicole Kidman, a successful, ball-breaking TV exec, is fired from her job after a disgruntled reality show contestant goes berserk. Her husband, hoping to rebuild their marriage, moves the family to the luxury Stepford estate in Connecticut. All the other wives on the estate (with the exceptions of Bette Midler and Glen Close's characters) are sugary-sweet blonde bimbo stereotypes running to do their husbands' slightest bidding. Yet all is not as it seems.The new version of Stepford Wives is glossy, antiseptic, and well-acted. Whilst possible to engage intellectually with it, there is no pretence of unfolding a story with gradual suspense ? rather it is a tribute to the original concept with a few added layers.(Spoilers follow)It transpires that the wives at Stepford have been ?improved' by means of nano-chips inserted in the brain, turning them into virtual robots (in the earlier film adaptation, they were given drugs). All of them were formerly high-powered successful women. Now they are more interested in kitchen recipes than intllectually challenging subjects. The men have remote control devices to fine tune the behaviour of their wives. The original idea was maybe that women should revolt against the wife-cook-and-bottle-washer image, the dye-blonde that was always docile, less successful than her husband, less intelligent, lived only to make him happy (a sort of ?50s stereotype). But the new version not only asks if women should really accept such a dumbed down role, it asks if men should be dumb enough to want it. It also turns the idea on its gender-specific head when we discover that Glen Close is not a Stepford Wife at all, but the evil mastermind behind the whole village, an ex- genetic engineer. She made the original ?Stepford Husband' (Christopher Walken) as a man that other men would look up to and listen to ? and, of course, give large amounts of money to. This raises the spectre of women who seek ?Stepford Husbands' ? ie traditionally subordinated males whose primary purpose is hunter-gatherer-provider.What the original is really saying is that to seek a standardised person in any sense or form is shortsighted and results in disaster (the gender issues raised by the original concept are merely some of the manifestations.) It's a form of onanism, treating people not as ends in themselves but as means to an end (usually our end). The starting scene (of TV reality-shows that fail when they are too successful), also lays the foundation for us to equate any seeming ?perfection' with ?Stepfordness'. As Kidman's character remarks later on, perfection isn't natural, the perfect marriage involves much more. The film is released by Paramount Pictures, and some people have observed that many executives and filmmakers there seem to have been replaced by Stepford replicants: perhaps it is to be expected when we get an audience-pandering finale. The (deliberate?) lack of emotional engagement possibly means that the people who really enjoy what the film has to say don't need a saccharine-coated ending, whereas the ones who need to be reassured by such devices as formulaic denouements will hardly feel satisfied as they have been given a ?Stepford treatment' film - such self-parody will probably have been lost on pure entertainment seekers.Perhaps the next re-make will substitute religion for drugs or nano-chips? The men will defend some repressive religion such as modern day Islam or Christianity ("Hey, Islam isn't that bad, lots of women LIKE being treated that way.") The sexual stereotypes, women who are just there to please their husbands, are particularly prevalent in religion . . .· Eph. 5:22-25 "Wives, submit yourselves unto your own husbands, as unto the Lord. For the husband is the head of the wife, even as Christ is the head of the church: and he is the saviour of the body. Therefore as the church is subject unto Christ, so let the wives be to their own husbands in every thing. Husbands, love your wives, even as Christ also loved the church, and gave himself for it" · Col. 3:18-19 "Wives, submit yourselves unto your own husbands, as it is fit in the Lord. Husbands, love your wives, and be not bitter against them."· 1 Pet. 3:1 "Likewise, ye wives, be in subjection to your own husbands; that, if any obey not the word, they also may without the word be won by the conversation of the wives;" · 1 Pet. 3:7-8 "Likewise, ye husbands, dwell with them according to knowledge, giving honour unto the wife, as unto the weaker vessel, and as being heirs together of the grace of life; that your prayers be not hindered."This type of brainwashing has resulted in a male-orientated definition of sex where sex = penetration (as the Hite Report pointed out, penetration is simply the most convenient way for men, not women, to achieve orgasm). Sex is then used as a controlling factor for men to either physically dominate their wives/partners into being Stepford Wives or for women to manipulate their men into being Stepford Husbands. The lack of recognition for a woman's basic physiology eventually produces a backlashStepford Wives has been passed over as an idea addressing yesterday's problems. But, as feminist philosopher Janet Radcliffe pointed out, when feminism achieves its apparent aims by means of a backlash against men (as exemplified in the early part of this film, women rising to dominance) then that creates the same seeds in men to fight back and put women back into a subordinate position. Often when this has happened, women have achieved their ends by a sort of masculinisation, emulating male characteristics in order to ?beat them at their own game'. The more permanent solution is for women to excel not by imitating men but by strengthening their own feminine characteristics. One of the attractions of the current movie for an actor of the calibre of Nicole Kidman must surely be the opportunity for exploring the many different ways a woman can develop her strength in a single character. She goes from (masculinised) TV Exec, to (imitation ? as she only pretends to undergo the procedure) blonde bimbo Stepford wife, to woman of strong and balanced emotional sensitivity. In the final incarnation as it were, she rescues her husband from himself, reminding him how love is an emotion he cannot experience fully with a robot, and then appears on television again as a more balanced individual than before, eschewing the modern obsession with perfection and promoting tolerance and communication as a road to success.See Stepford Wives and use it as a stepping stone to question, discuss and examine how far the attitudes iconised in the film are still prevalent, not just in the most obvious sense but in the many layers of society where one person manipulates another, or is treats someone as little more than a means to an end.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I warn anyone who is yet to see it that this review will spoil it for you.Hey, does anybody know what year this latest version is set in? And they are philanderers, whilst their men are faithful. There is open and shameless acceptance of the humiliation of men. While the feminist movement began with a desire for education, emancipation and equal career prospects, the 'masculist' movement appears to have begun with regaining touch - with their masculinity, with their boyishness and with their selfishness. Such is the extent of evolution at this stage, or corruption at this stage, that the men are just jokes. They cannot see past their own long-compromised egos. So, when Glenn Close creates her cyber-monster male chauvinist robot husband, he quickly finds a cache of disciples among these men. And so short is the vision of these 'men', that they happily seek to convert their wives into air-headed pot-scrubbing sex kittens.There is a question, asked by Joanna Eberhart just before she descends into the pit to be 'Stepford-ised'. She asks her husband if his new robotic woman can say she loves him. Glenn Close's cyber-monster interjects: 'in 37 languages'. What else would you expect from him? But then, the real question: 'Will she MEAN it?'Well, what will we have if we reach the stage where men are virtually helpless? Can a male truly love a woman to whom he is completely subservient, not by choice, but because of how society has finally structured itself? We can walk away from seeing this film with some enrichment, even if it's not direct. There is a message to both the sexes in it.Nicole Kidman excels, as the caricature of the ultimate 'female chauvinist pig' at the beginning, as the growing character in Stepford who questions and refines her role in the family, and finally as the voice of reason. 		<shortDescription>Scary - for all the wrong reasons</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The original version of The Stepford Wives may have been no masterpiece, but it was Citizen Kane compared with this drek!Unsure of whether to play it safe or go out on a limb, to play up the anachronisms of a badly outdated concept or bring it into line with the 21st century, director Frank Oz has fumbled around incoherently. And he actually manages to include something to offend everyone - not in a Michael Moore, politically pointed way, but in a monstrously inept display of sheer excess.The film is ugly, pointless, humourless and in the final washup, completely worthless. There is something seriously wrong with your movie when Bette Midler is your most restrained cast member. Indeed, Nicole Kidman offers what may very well be the most appalling performance she has ever offered in a (to say the least) bumpy career. She makes no attempt at all to find a recognisably human foundation for her character, playing it instead as a broad caricature. And since the film's thesis is that the loss of humanity for a 'perfect society' the film virtually crumples into dust amidst Kidman's embarrassing mugging. I know there are people out there who maintain she is some sort of goddess, but sorry guys, you are worshipping the wrong diva.Bette Midler provides a few smiles along the way, but she is way below par. At least she seems to know how wretched the material is, which is more than we can say for Glenn Close. She actually makes Kidman look subdued.The film's finale is muddled and unclear. Are the Stepford Wives robots or women with computer chips in their brains? Do men in America really desire overdressed zombies for wives? Do men even think about repressing women any more? Undoubtedly some do, but the notion that an entire community would launch itself into this bizarre scheme is ridiculous, even by this film's modest standards.There must be some virtue in this film. How about a triumph of style over content. Alas no. The film rambles along in sub-par Tim Burton style. How about making a few bucks at the box office? Alas yes, though at least the public has belatedly seen the light.Awful film making. I don't mind if I never see anybody connected with it ever again - Midler excepted.</fullReview>

		<username>John Nail (ascheland)</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Robotic Approach to Cute Comedy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm generally wary of remakes, especially when studios can do nothing better with the original material than update it with current stars and a dumbed-down script. Remaking "The Stepford Wives" as a comedy was an inspired idea, but the resulting movie is not. The potential to turn Ira Levin's thriller into a black comedy is there, but in the hands of screenwriter Paul ("In &amp; Out") Rudnick and director Frank Oz, "The Stepford Wives" is light n' amusing--and muddled--fluff. Rudnick is quick with the one-liners, but seems unsure with the plot, which is filled with contradictions. For instance, are these women being turned into robots or do they just have a chip implanted in their brains? (The movie presents both explanations with no attempt to reconcile them.) And the tacked-on epilogue appears to be just that: tacked on.However, if you're willing to overlook flaws in its plotting and obviousness in its humor, there are two reasons to see "The Stepford Wives": Bette Midler and Glenn Close. Midler is Midler, but that's exactly what her fans want. And following such un-funny comic vehicles as "Isn't She Great" and "Drowning Mona," it was nice to enjoy her in a movie again. Close is perfect as Claire Wellington, the most domineering of Stepford's wives, simultaneously creepy and hilarious. An honorable mention goes to Roger Bart, who plays one half of Stepford's token gay couple as if channeling the spirit of the late Paul Lynde. Top-billed stars Matthew Broderick and Nicole Kidman have their moments, but they're easily upstaged by Midler and Close.By keeping your expectations low, there are enough laughs in this comic take "The Stepford Wives" to make it worth seeing, if only at matinee prices.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>politically-correct silliness over satire</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's certainly a premise that's relevant for today's audiences, maybe even more so than when the original "Stepford Wives" was released in 1975, but director Frank Oz and scripter Paul Rudnick transform Ira Levin's allegorical tale of male chauvanism into a hit-or-miss screwball comedy. The film lacks any of the mystery (one of the two key twists is revealed early on), suspense, or horror of the original (which, while a flawed film, at least had some convictions), though many of the scenes are lovingly reproduced (the concluding grocery store sequence, por ejemplo). Nicole Kidman fits well as the lead, a cold-hearted woman looking to jump-start her life, possessing the right blend of sex appeal and comic timing; Bette Midler steals the show as the only other non-conformed female in Stepford. But despite the faithfulness to the original film (transposed somewhat uncomfortably into the realm of comedy), the ending is a shameless cop-out of politically-correct silliness, defying the downbeat sense of total male domination over the female gender which gave the original an eerie resonance--the climax plays it far too safe, and ultimately ruins any modern-day prophecy this film would have been looking to convey (if at all).5/10</fullReview>

Most fail to realize however, that it's more satire than either comedy or horror. Such *nasty* satire at that, that the humor becomes almost serious by the end of the film, and we should be left realizing that the first 15 minutes are meant to be as disturbing as the last 15 minutes.HELLLLLOOOOOO America! Stepford is supposed to be us. Worse, it *is* us. Put down the popcorn and think about it, because despite the laughs, this is a very serious movie.The original film was made during a time of radical social change in the sexual arena. Feminism and sex roles were revolutionary concepts, and were thus best presented in a horror film, with the main character teetering on the brink of the unknown, unsure of the fate which awaited her, until she was eventually consumed by her existence.This film is made during yet another time of social change: although we have largely accepted that gender roles and stereotypes are idiotic, we nonetheless cling to them because we don't know what else to do. Take a look at our country's division over same sex marriage- do you think it's an accident that they put a same sex couple in the forefront of this film?Stepford *is* America, a land where we have two kinds of people: men and women. The men act like men, and the women act like women. Anyone not fitting into the pattern will be *forced* to fit the pattern, because deviations . . . well . . . they're just not very nice.Are you still laughing? I hope you like irony with your popcorn.</fullReview>

		<username>rogerdarlington</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A movie to miss</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Why do they do it? Can't Hollywood craft some new storylines instead of cloning endless remakes? As if three television sequels was not enough. Of course, if you're going to reshoot a film, you have to have some different angles but everything different about this new version of the 1975 work by Bryan Forbes is a mistake. The advances in cinematic technology could have made for some great visual tricks, but the opportunity was squandered. More seriously, whereas the original movie was a scary thriller with a strong feminist message, this regurgitation is simply played for laughs and - especially in the twist at the end - finishes up betraying the cause of liberation and equality. In 1975, one could well imagine men challenged by the increasing independence of women wishing to return to the halcyon days of the 1950s, but surely the idea that men - even podgy, middle-aged Americans - would want to turn the clock back 50 years to cake-making, crinoline-wearing dumb blondes is just too tired an idea to be credible. The men in this movie are truly awful - Matthew Broderick has lost his spark and Christopher Walken sleep walks through yet another evil personna - with only Roger Bart, as the camp end of a gay partnership, relieving the boredom. The actresses - those who aren't playing robots anyway - give strong performances with Nicole Kidman displaying a wonderful variety of facial expressions as she is sacked from the TV network and Glenn Close proving eerily icy (sadly the wonderful Bette Midler is underused). But nothing can prevent this from being a movie to miss.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Doomed Housewives trapped in Desperate Kingdom of a Twisted Disneyland of Love</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There's but very little left of the wonderful modern Gothic Ira Levin wrote. Now, Stepford is not a small suburban village: it has become a gated community in Fairfield County. Joanna Eberhart, the doomed and horrified housewife of the original tale (as played by Katharine Ross in 1975) is now a ball-breaking ambitious and androgynous TV exec. The motivations behind EVERYTHING change. I can't reveal all, because I'd be spoiling it, but let's just say that this is quite a different Stepford... see, this is sort of a "Love" story.It' at times creepy... but it feels more like the story of a woman trapped in the desperate kingdom of a twisted Disneyland of Love...As for the acting: Ms. Kidman plays it to the hilt: she's having a ball, but it must have been exhausting. Bette Midler is DIVA (yes, well, we knew that) and she won't let you forget about it. Glenn Close is a hoot: she can camp it up and she does, whereas Christopher Walken gives a wonderfully adroit performance and Matthew (yum, yum) Broderick is actually kinda lovable as a some sort of Ferris Bueller grown up.Roger Bart plays a new element in the plot: a gay character. That is nice, but I think Rudnick took it all on the Screaming Queen stereotypes: sometimes it's like watching Paula Prentiss in male drag. I mean, it's very very very OVER THE TOP, and David Marshall Grant plays a smarmy gay Stepford Hubby... which is a not-uninteresting change.The thing that will bother many is the ending. And lemme tell you... it will be completely understandable. You can tell there were re shoots, and that at one point, the writer lost his way. I mean, I love the theme, I've read the book several times ever since childhood, and I like the cast, specially Kidman and Broderick... but I feel that Rudnick, in pursue of something that is never made clear (laughs? shrieks?)has Stepfordized a story that didn't need to be so.Oh well... being such a devoted fan of the original story this felt like having a Key Lime Pie that is not runny enough (according to the glorious Brigid Berlin, key lime pie MUST be runny)but still it is edible and tasty. I am guessing this is not going to be a mass blockbuster -- not running against Shrek and that kid with the broom- but it definitely has cult possibilities. And you know? It's like a guilty pleasure... like watching reruns of The Swan while gorging on Ben &amp; Jerry's Rocky Road.Oh, and about Faith Hill... let's just say she gets a lot of good laughs. In hindsight, I think that is an asset on the film: I actually found myself letting go and laughing heartily quite a bit. But oh, those opening credits!! Phew!!!Well, this version is as camp as tents up in a row. So, like Bobbie Markowe used to say in the original book, regarding her sparkling Stepford kitchen: "Go, look."Still, it could be worse. Scooby Doo, anyone?Oh, and speaking of Miss Kidman... whenever you get the chance RUN RUN RUN to see DOGVILLE, by Lars Von Trier, starring Kidman and an impressive cast of pros. It WILL change the way you look at cinema,forever...6/10</fullReview>

		<username>Elswet</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>The Stepford Wives Remix.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This review contains spoilers for both this movie, and the original, "The Stepford Wives."Even a horror comedy can have a cohesive story line. This story is more than convoluted. It's bastardized. It's completely lost. Don't get me wrong, I LOVE this movie. It's fun and it's funny, and there are some strong moments between Kidman and Broderick. Everyone put in good performances. But the direction was non-existent. I mean, come on. They didn't transplant the brains into robotic bodies. They simply couldn't make up their minds as to what they wanted to DO. So we ended up with a horrible mess. A fun mess, but still a mess. I totally believe that whole transplant device was invented after the fact and was never a part of the original storyline. I read the reviews and movie news and this movie was plagued with problems. Script problems, personality problems, emotional problems, set problems; you name it. And it definitely shows. In the original movie (don't know, don't care who saw it), the wives were murdered in pure cold blood, and replaced by robots. In the remake, they wanted a lighter theme than the cold-blooded murder of these women, so after filming the women like they were going to be robots, they began shooting them like they were altered, rather than replaced. In the remake, they wanted desperately for the women to be able to be "changed back," to be given their minds again. They wanted to redeem the male species of these atrocities of the original work, and thereby, they ruined their whole effort.The story got more than lost between all the arguing between producers and staff, etc. and this hodge-podge is what we got stuck with. They cut the Bette Middler/lawnmower scene because they changed their story line half way through the movie and no longer wanted to portray her as THAT robotic, but more "altered" instead. It didn't work. Once they gave us a human ATM machine who was controlled with her own personal remote, it was too late to turn around and sell the audience on the whole watered-down chips-in-the-brain premise (which was lame as all H3LL anyway), and expect us to buy it. So basically, they just said screw it, slapped it together and handed it to the fans. The movie made no sense because the movie was the first half of one movie, and the second half of a totally different movie. They should have sacrificed some of the low-brow humor for a more intelligent pass on this film, IMO. Had they had the cajones to actually "ring the bell" (by killing off the wives as in the original) with this attempt, the Bette Middler/Lawnmower scene could have been left in and this movie would have made a lot more fans happy. The one thing I *did* find refreshing was the inclusion of the first gay Stepford couple. It's about time Hollywood moved in to include everyone and not just the purely redneck, white bread, mainstream, over-christianized Americans. Nice touch. Too bad the rest of the movie wasn't as progressive.Although I highly recommend watching the original over this one, it's still a fun endeavor, however, and rates a 6.1/10 from...the Fiend :.</fullReview>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Not even a nice place to visit, let alone one to live in.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The original take on "The Stepford Wives" led to three TV movie sequels - "Return of the Stepford Wives," "The Stepford Children" and (inevitably) "The Stepford Husbands." This new version makes no impact at all, so we'll be spared any followups... especially since "The Stepford Children" (in which Barbara Eden and Don Murray come to Stepford with their kids, and Mum becomes suspicious about how incredibly polite and well-behaved the kids are), the only one I've seen, is better than this movie. That's right, Jeannie and Sid from "Knots Landing" are better value than Nicole Kidman and Matthew Broderick.In Frank Oz's latest attempt at making a good comedy - or indeed a good MOVIE - that doesn't involve all-out fantasy ("Dirty Rotten Scoundrels" has its admirers, but I'm not one of them), he and writer Paul Rudnick apply a heavy hand on the satire from the word go, with stressed-out TV executive Kidman (who I sincerely hope is better as Samantha in the "Bewitched" movie than she is here) kicked out of her job, supportive husband Broderick resigning and the whole family moving to the peaceful town of Stepford, a place which so obviously signposts that Something Is Not Right Here that only a blind man could miss it. Everybody's slotted in place, and the "real" people here are no more flesh-and-blood than the Stepford wives themselves; the network presentation that begins the movie - Kidman's character winds up being fired when a man who was humiliated by his wife on one of her reality TV shows, having shot said wife and several of her boyfriends (!), crashes the meeting and opens fire on her - is actually considerably more interesting than what the movie's really about, the shades of what happened on Jenny Jones's show notwithstanding; this viewer wished the movie had been about what led up to that moment, which would have spared us comedy that isn't funny (except for a scene where Kidman, Roger Bart and Bette Midler sneak into Faith Hill's house and hear her having really great sex), attempts to turn the movie serious that fail completely because it hasn't come across as light in the first place, and a climax that turns the movie from a failed commentary on the roles of men and women into yet another perfection-is-bad-and-fake-flaws-are-good-and-real lecture. (On this showing, give me perfection any day.)The cast is mostly thrown away - Midler stands out, but only because she never seems to take what she's doing seriously (and yes, in this case that is a criticism) - and while there are a sprinkling of good moments like the aerobics scene set to "A Fifth of Beethoven" and some amusing lines (Kidman's character on hearing that one of the husbands used to work for AOL: "Is that why the women are so slow?"), the movie's entirely free of any kind of effect, other than a deadening one. If only that husband had shot her in the beginning.</fullReview>

Nicole Kidman dispatches her role as the effete TV executive with ease. She stretches from sterile TV star to manic fish-out-of-water suburban mom to perfect housewife without missing any beats. You get the impression this is child's play for her. Of course this movie is beneath the A-list cast: Close, Kidman, Walken, and Broderick (maybe Midler is B-list with A-list credentials?). I suppose they were all gathered up to create a worthy remake, but it needs more, much more.Thinking back to "Biloxi Blues," I was hoping for more interaction between Walken and Broderick, but, alas, 'twas not to be. Still, it's always fun to see Walken on screen. I for one am glad he rarely turns down a role. Close is probably the best of all on-screen: she tackles the role of the ultimate housewife-gone-mad with great alacrity.So, go ahead and wait for the rental--but make sure you rent something else too. You'll have a short night otherwise.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Unnecessary Remake</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>`The Stepford Wives' is kind of a slow story. Slow because it seems to take a ridiculously long time for the major characters to figure out that something is amiss in good ol' Stepford, CT. Considering that the plodding story line is coupled with lackluster dialogue, an unremarkable soundtrack, underwhelming cinematography and the complete non-exploitation of some remarkably beautiful women we come up with a pretty disappointing remake. Actually, the movie starts well. Joanna Eberhard (Nicole Kidman ) is a relentlessly aggressive TV network executive and it is fun to watch as she introduces a new show at a company function. And for all intents and purposes the show downshifts as she subsequently suffers depression (burnout?) after things don't go exactly as she planned. Thus her husband Walter (Matthew Broderick) quits his job and whisks her off to suburbia to heal both her and their marriage.  But things are odd in Stepford, and we find perfect ? and lovely - wives matched with normal looking guys (shading toward unattractive, actually). As Joanna comes out of her funk she, paired primarily with new friends Bobbie (Better Midler) and Roger (Roger Bart), begins to suspect something is amiss. But even if we had never heard of the original film or book it is hardly a revelation when the trio cracks the case. The only truly funny scene in the whole film occurs when Walter shows his wife (while she is in her darkest hour) a picture one of their children drew. It is so insensitive it is flat hilarious and is the only real surprise of the movie. Otherwise this stacks up to be a 6-8 chuckle film.Disappointments: Faith Hill's appearance is brief and unrevealing (in an acting sense); Christopher Walken and Glenn Close (the unofficial, it seems, leaders of the community) are not challenged very much; truth be told this is no where near as sexy or dark a film as trailers indicated it would be. Conclusion: another in a long line of unnecessary Hollywood remakes. Rating: 2-1/2 stars.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Promising at first, but collapsing after 45 minutes</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This remake offers a good cast, a promising team and first and foremost a very intriguing plot. Unfortunately the filmmakers could not entirely decide whether to make a sci fi thriller like the movie from the seventies or a comedy. Then the movie sets his tone introducing Nicole Kidman as a superpopular TV-host (Mr. Kidman was never colder as a character)who bathes herself in the success of her cruel real-time TV shows. After getting fired for basically ruining peoples lives with inhumanly disgusting shows she finds herself in the superclean and somewhat unreal little town of Stepford. While Kidman's character is very well developed, her husband's is not. Matthew Broderick fails to persuade us that he is a true heartbreaker and a great companion for a career-woman such as Kidman. Later we do not want to buy that Broderick's character is turned around by the macho-attitudes of the Stepford-husbands, he is too nice for that.In the third act the whole thing collapses because the filmmakers do not succeed in turning this comedy into a really frightening thriller. This story is interesting because of the horrible truth being discovered at the end. Instead of leaving us with an open end which would make us moan the characters we fell in love with before (Kidman, Bette Midler) this movie delivers us a poor explanation that takes away the horror - and the horror is the most attractive part of this story in the first place. THE STEPFORD WIVES wants to be a comedy and a thriller, wants to be sarcastic, full of secrets, exiting, a little heart worming and shocking - and is nothing of the above completely. A straight thriller with this cast and crew would have made a great film. OK, I also had some laughs...only to bow my head during the last 15 minutes!</fullReview>

		<date>2004-06-16</date>
		<username>J.D. Corcoran</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Take off the rose-colored glasses; the original was NOT better, just different.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Frank Oz directs a tongue-in-rouged-cheek redux that stumbles a tad as it doesn't quite commit fully to either a black comedy or an over-the-top satire, doesn't quite reach "thriller" quality, doesn't quite step up to the plate. BUT it's inevitable: When remakes are released of 'classic' movies ? from Ocean's 11 to the Stepford Wives ? we don our rose-colored glasses and wax poetic about how much better the original was. In this case: Wrong. Different, yes; better, no. This remake is true to the premise of the original, but takes a fresh, skewed approach, going for camp and parody where the original was a drama; going for speedy, full-bodied character development right out of the gate where the original dragged its characters along woefully, with hangdog looks and dour expressions substituted for dialogue that told their backstory. Uber-urban couple Joanna and Walter move to the Connecticut 'Burbs this timeafter Joanna has a complete nervous breakdown when her big city career implodes. She's a power woman on a power trip, and her cord gets unplugged big time. So, Joanna's wrassling her own identity crisis when she meets the blissful, beautiful, subdued society of Stepford. Despite an peculiar speech pattern, Nicole Kidman is splendid as Joanna, as she struggles to come up for air in Stepford. Instead of enshrining Joanna as the Feminist Goddess fighting deadhead domesticity as in the original, this version shows her warts and all, and the audience seemed to feel she got what she deserved initially. There is a sweetness in Joanna trying to find her softer feminine side, her nesting and nurturing instincts, even when her hyper type-A personality kicks into gear, resulting in hundreds of cupcakes screaming 'compulsive overachiever.' Her new best friend Bobbie (Bette Midler) is, refreshingly, NOT a sympathetic character, and (perhaps inadvertently) Director Oz has crossed her with Miss Piggy, as an egocentric, combative, acerbic writer who wallows in a slob-sty lifestyle with her pig of a husband, the ever-idiotic Jon Lovitz. The injection of a gay couple into Stepford is an interesting twist, and Faith Hill is utter perfection as the Stepford wife with her wiring going awry. Christopher Walken is a bit underused, but as always, reliably weird and oddly ominous as the head honcho of the sexist men's club, which property is dead on from the original movie. Matthew Broderick is, as Joanna succinctly states, her 'goofy Walter,' all wide-eyed and struggling to assert himself as his emasculated testosterone gets refueled in Happy Man Land. Glenn Close is a wasp-waisted dervish as the untiring, endlessly organizational, insidiously irritating Claire, who leads the women of Stepford in their goosesteps. The good news is that, unlike the sluggish, drably introspective original, this Stepford Wives is glamorous and richly imbued with vibrancy. The bad news is that -- OK, social commentary opinion ahead, feel free to tune out -- sadly, the kneejerk ridiculing and debasing of all things traditional and June-Cleaver-like is overt and heavy-handed in Hollywood, and wrapping it up in glitzy, star-laden movies like this may be "fun" but it has more than a bit of a nasty, mean spirited undertone. In today's world of latchkey kids, rampant divorce, domestic violence, lack of honor and integrity, shirked personal responsibility, crude and rude antisocial behavior, etc., we could use MORE family tranquility, dedicated husbands and wives, and respect for the sanctuary of a welcoming home with an unselfish, loving, committed father and mother who embrace the most important job in the world: raising better children which leads ultimately to a better world. The burgeoning trend of 'stay at home Moms' in America seems to be an uplifting, promising development in improving the quality of society through improving the quality of family. It's unfortunate if movies like The Stepford Wives slap these folks down for their 'old fashioned' values.</fullReview>
	</review>

If they're humans with chips in their brains then what's with the sparking ears, growing breasts and the ATM machine (who refills that cashbox anyway?). Just because they've made it into a (supposed) comedy doesn't mean they can abandon all sense of internal logic.As for that, it tries really hard to be a comedy but, except for 5 or 6 snappy one-liners, ends up as a vaguely embarrassing piece of slapstick. Unlike the classic original, there's no tension since 99% of the audience already know the Stepford secret, and it can't even claim to be satirising anything. The 70's version was a comment on people's reactions to the burgeoning feminist movement, but what is there to satirise here - reality TV? Lame.Over the top performances (except for Broderick who sleepwalks through his underwritten role) and too many plot holes make this just another of the countless pointless remakes which unwisely choose not to take the original seriously.Please, please Hollywood: By all means keep on making remakes, but why not remake some s****y movies and turn them into something good, rather than the other way round.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-06-14</date>
		<username>anhedonia</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Could've used a lot more spark and subtlety</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>(WARNING: THIS REVIEW CONTAINS A FEW SPOILERS)Maybe it's just the wrong time to remake "The Stepford Wives." Or, perhaps the story isn't conducive to being remade. After all, Ira Levin's 1972 novel is a horror story, as was Bryan Forbes' 1975 film. Now, 19 years later, with the term "Stepford Wife" so enmeshed in our lexicon, making a horror film would certainly prove futile.That's clearly the reason writer Paul Rudnick and director Frank Oz opted to turn Levin's work into a campy comedy. We already know the Stepford wife's secret (unless, of course, you've had your head buried in the sand all these years), so Rudnick wastes no time in revealing the inner workings of one very early into the film.But if you're resorting to comedy, it better be funny. And that's where this "Stepford Wives" fails miserably. There's no doubt Rudnick can write hilariously funny stuff. "In and Out" had genuinely funny moments and his "If You Ask Me" column in Premiere magazine (under the nom de plume Libby Gelman-Waxner) often features witty, brilliant zingers.Unfortunately, none of that's evident in "The Stepford Wives," which has a few chuckles, a couple of good lines. I'll admit I laughed when one of the Stepford husbands announces he worked for AOL and Joanna whips back, "Is that why the women are so slow?" And Claire's plea for a screwdriver was nicely timed, too. Scenes begin with tremendous promise, then, like a malfunctioning Stepford wife, peter out, lose their spark. Bobbie and Joanna's break-in of the Men's Association proves to be merely a tease, for nothing even remotely funny or terrifying comes from it. Granted, the Stepford ATM was awfully intriguing - if only there were many more such bits of inspired novelty.Subtle, this film is not. And this film needed subtlety. Instead, almost everything and everyone is over-the-top, from the faux reality shows to several performances, including those by Glenn Close, Roger Bart and Christopher Walken. Understated humor would have worked wonders. Too bad Rudnick didn't see the need for it.Kidman's always a radiant screen presence. She has a good grasp of Joanna, but her role, like the film, feels flimsy. Singer Faith Hill might very well prove to be a good actress some day, but here she's given next to nothing to do. Bette Midler has a few clever lines, but she, too, is playing someone we've seen numerous times from her.One huge problem is the nonexistent chemistry between Kidman and Matthew Broderick, a smart actor who seems woefully miscast. They just don't play well off each other. They both look as if they'd rather be somewhere else, doing something else. Their scenes need zing; instead, we get zip.Then there's the new ending. I am a fan of the original ending. I liked its pessimism. I have no problem with Rudnick coming up with a new twist ending. Alas, it's not unpredictable and at the end, we're left wondering if this is the best Rudnick and Oz could devise."The Stepford Wives" proved to be a disappointment, despite boasting an enormously talented cast. It's almost as if this film unintentionally became a Stepford version of the original film and novel.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2004-06-14</date>
		<username>canadude</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>The Stepford Film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This recent remake (God, I'm starting to despise that word) of the 1975 thriller is a comedy bordering on slapstick. It's like a bad joke told by a guy who heard the joke originally as a serious story, but he did not get it or like it. The new "Stepford Wives" is an affront to the original film, the original book, the themes covered in both and yet another symptom of the plague of remakes polluting our society these stark, dismal days.It's all too bad, because the film had one hell of a cast. Nicole Kidman, Matthew Broderick, Glenn Close, Bette Midler, Jon Lovitz and last, but not least Christopher Walken star in this tale of forced, mechanized perfection, but their talents pretty much go to waste. (Not to mention Faith Hill who has less than two or three non-memorable lines).So where did it all go wrong? Frank Oz is hardly an incapable director and the topic could have possibly been interesting. The idea of men building a utopian society by turning their wives into subservient fembots definitely had social resonance in the 70s. Women were still struggling in a society of "evil male oppression," and film and literature addressed the problems of conformity and death of individualism quite prominently at the time. However, times change and the last thirty years changed quite a bit. Women, while some still claim to experience this "evil male oppression," are generally free to pursue whatever career goals they wish and conformity is such a problem right now that no one talks about it. Consequently, it's quite clear that the remake of "The Stepford Wives," if it still wanted to be the incisive satire akin to the original, should adjust its story to the contemporary social climate.Well, it does and it doesn't. It does in the sense that the film is made strictly for money, reassures the audience instead of disturbing them, and changes the terrifying ending of the original into a glossed-over, resolution bordering on the Stepford definition of perfection. It's a sell-out film and it's ending is designed to please everyone - women and men alike. It's simply politically correct. And garishly so.Thus, concurrently, the film doesn't succeed as a satire at all. It doesn't take sides in fear of offending somebody. While it starts off with a noncommittal comment on women exploiting men on TV, it quickly returns to the feminist-deliberation-style of its predecessor. Nicole Kidman's Joanna lands in Stepford and finds women abhorrently friendly and perfect. However, by its end, "The Stepford Wives" turns around and tells us it's not about women oppressing men, men oppressing women, or gay men oppressing other gay men - it's about the terror of the traditionalism and conservativeness of the 1950s. Yeah. The shocking revelation at the end of the new "The Stepford Wives" has nothing to do with the themes it purportedly explores. It's simply a bad joke about a time that has come and gone even before the original "The Stepford Wives" was made. This type of ending, of course, has absolutely no appeal, satirical or not, nowadays. It is completely absurd and mocking - as if to tell you "Ha ha, this is what your dumb-ass gets for sitting through this film!"And no one gets hurt, or everyone gets hurt equally. The men are reassured at the end that if they stick by their woman, more ambitious or less ambitious than they are, they are "real men" and therefore are good. Men who feel threatened by their wives or girlfriends are weak and exhibit signs of extreme insecurity, career-related or sexual. Finally, women and gay men, as long as they are witty and don't surrender their neuroses and lack of happiness to make life easier for their partners, are, in their own little way, perfect."The Stepford Wives" thus shows us nothing more than current politically-correct attitudes held by the majority of our society. It doesn't seek to question why these attitudes exist in the first place, or, if they are accurate, what motivates the social forces in these particular ways. Thus "The Stepford Wives" is a symptom of the very thing which it claims to be about - a sign of dangerous conformity and fear of offending others. And, I am just about sick of it.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Hackneyed History vs. Horror Hokum</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There is a disturbing trend toward rewriting or altogether forgetting historical cinematic perspective. The original "Stepford Wives", appropriately dramatic in intent, concentrated on a single character (played graciously by Katharine Ross) gradually uncovering a frightening and deadly chauvinistic plot against the women of an upscale Connecticut community. The 00's version seems to rely solely on the current one-trick pony of coarse, campy humor, with little to no regard for traditional storyline. Add to this a pretentious parade of identifiable pop icons (Kidman, Midler, etc.) in order to sell this flimsy film and you have an audience whose sensibilities have been bodysnatched and brain-eaten by rampaging, robotic redux. Alliteration aside, there is nothing new under the sun NOR on the silver screen!</fullReview>

		<date>2004-06-03</date>
		<username>dumpster_cakes</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>I, Robot...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>(spoilers)2004, the era of PMS (Post-Martha Stewart) seems ripe for a dark-edged satire of sex roles like Ira Levin's 'The Stepford Wives', filtered through three decades of suburban flight, backlashes against feminist backlashes, and rampant consumerism fed by fear of almost everything. Add to that, Levin's story added a distinct layer of body-horror and phobic preoccupation with cleanliness which Bryan Forbes's original 1975 adaptation cleverly exploited---in its honey-glowed cinematography and women whose flowered dresses melted into the black hole of a sun, the whole film played like the most horrific deodorant commercial imaginable. The safest of suburban retreats (perfectly manicured gardens and gleamingly clean supermarkets) became threatening vistas of unease. It was dark-humored, but at its core, it was pure horror played out in sunny kitchens and picket fences---the diary of a mad housewife gone animatronic. It's an unnerving, underrated classic, up there with Kaufman's 'Invasion of the Body Snatchers' remake as a dystopic vision of modern society kept under control through homicidal identity-theft.Zombified, robotized suburbanites who compulsively yearn for an unthreatening social structure regardless of the cost? G.W. Bush's America is the looking-glass realization of these visions, where the importance of appearance and the desire for a simple, uncomplicated society override the messiness of social pluralism and whatever else lies beyond the cul-de-sac's driveway. Overseen by a biting satirist like John Waters, a 'Stepford' remake might have had a chance at exploiting the inherent irony of updating this story to 2004, despite Waters's propensity for narrative crudeness. Or perhaps the two Todds might have succeeded at mining the rich potential in upping the story's ante in an era fueled by SUVs and internet shopping; either Solondz's malcontent nastiness or Haynes's tone-perfect genre revisionism would have served the concept well. What we get is Frank Oz and scenarist Paul Rudnick who offer up a remake of sorts but the results are a very distant echo from the 1975 original. The emphasis is funny---not satire or sneaky-but-horrific funny---but broad, cartoon-speed funny. In this version, Joanna is transformed from a housewife/photographer to a show biz exec who is punished with expulsion after one of her reality show participants goes postal at a network meeting.Joanna and her nebbishy husband (Matthew Broderick, duller than wet crackers) and their nondescript children move to Stepford where everything is 'House Beautiful'-perfect (although it's more perfect in an art-directed way than a suburban fantasy way, a further symptom of the film's cartoonishness). The tired, disheveled Joanna doesn't belong and her displacement is underscored when she bonds with two other outsiders, newcomers Bobbie (Bette Midler, funny but criminally underutilized) and Roger (Roger Bart as a swishy gay sweater queen---one could make the argument that the film contradicts whatever message it's offering about respecting individual differences by throwing in such a creaky stereotype, but since he's probably a stand-in for writer Rudnick, we'll give this plot point a pass). Joanna and company uncover the real reason behind the townswomen's preternatural obedience, sexual satisfaction with their nerdy husbands and preoccupation with home decorating. In the Martha/Queer Eye/Trading Places age of media-directed home improvement, it's hard to tell this is supposed to be so astounding but it's not the least of the film's failings that these contradictions and ironies aren't confronted. It's confounding as to what Oz and Rudnick were trying to accomplish with this remake. The basic scenario is too unnerving (or unnervingly real in light of contemporary mores) to function as a light comedy but that's exactly what the filmmakers serve up---the actors are given characters void of motivation or nuance, but are encouraged to play those non-characters at maximum speed and volume which after an hour of non-development, becomes unbearable to watch. Kidman might have survived had she been given the original Goldman script to work with, but with little help from Rudnick or Oz, she's left flailing; Joanna in the remake might have made sense if she had been a failure as a housewife or openly disdainful of the expectations placed on her as a newly minted suburban mom in a harshly lemon-pledged suburban hell. As it is, Joanna's mildly resentful of not fitting in, and there are one or two poorly drawn 'should we stay or should we go' arguments between her and Broderick's character. The 1975 film portrayed Joanna as a character skating along the borders of paranoia, persecution and uncorroborated suspicion---all that's now gone, replaced by a gimmicky comedic tone that goes for cheap laughs and blows the film's housewives-as-robots conceit in the first act. Midler and Bart are at least able to run with the film's Wile e. Coyote/Roadrunner comedic tone, but the film's weightlessness and inconsequence eventually swallows them up along with pros like Glenn Close and Chris Walken (proof that hiring an experienced, expensive cast can't save a bad script). Who needs character development and suspense where there's yucks to be had?'The Stepford Wives' (1975), while not a perfect film, was willing to peer into some dark and heady fantasies of identity displacement and suburban discontent in a era of shifting sex roles. Behind the perfect homes and pretty lawns was an unnervingly prescient look at the disappointment and anomie behind an impossibly idealized existence. 'The Stepford Wives' (2004) is an empty, soulless, mechanical film that, while technically accomplished, smacks of film-making-by-committee. Maybe it was made by the droids from the first film??</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>The Thrill is Gone...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>THE STEPFORD WIVES is Frank Oz' radical re-thinking of the 1975 classic about the mysterious town of Stepford where everything is not as neat and clean as it appears on the outside. In the original, Katherine Ross and Paula Prentiss played two relatively new citizens of Stepford, who desperately tried to figure out what was going on with the women of Stepford before falling under the "spell." In this remake, Nicole Kidman plays the Ross role, Joanna, who is now a high powered television executive who was fired from her job and had a nervous breakdown, who is brought to Stepford by her husband Walter (Matthew Broderick)to start a new life with her husband and children. Stepford has been updated for the new millennium: there is even a gay couple played by David Marshall Grant and Roger Bart (THE PRODUCERS; DESPERATE HOUSEWIVES), but it's the same old evil. Unfortunately, Oz plays all his cards right away and all the suspense of the original is drained away. In the original, there is some mystery as to what's going on with these men and women, but there is no mystery at all in this version...it's spelled out for us five minutes after Joanna and Walter arrive in Stepford. Not only is the mystery of the wives completely spelled out but the mystery of the Stepford Men's Association is laughable in this version...it comes off looking like a bunch of teenage boys sitting around drinking and playing video games. It also breaks my heart to say, as a huge Nicole Kidman fan, this is the first performance of hers that was really a disappointment for me. I found Kidman's performance to be broad and affected, somewhat like a Stepford wife before she is Stepford-ized. There are laughs to be found here and they come from Bette Midler, a joy in the Paula Prentiss role, Bart, Christopher Walken, and Glenn Close, who are perfection as Stepford's version of Welcome Wagon, all set against Oscar-worthy scenery and costumes, but next to the original classic...this is a pale , pale copy.</fullReview>
