		<username>Dennis Littrell</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>PC sellout</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The ending here is beyond redemption, but otherwise this remake of the 1975 film from the novel by Ira Levin is not (entirely) bad. It's a different sort of film than the original and probably would amuse (if not offend) Ira Levin if he were alive today. But to put it bluntly, the film is a sellout to politically correct mentalities and could quite easily be called "Revenge of the Stepford Wives." (Actually there was a TV movie from 1980 by that name that I haven't seen.) The original Stepford Wives starred Katharine Ross and Paula Prentiss and was a dark science fiction satire with only a few chuckles and a decidedly dark ending. This film is played mostly for laughs and is full of all sorts of silly (shall I say stupid?) updates, like having a gay couple instead of a black couple and making the wives (prior to their coming to Stepford) very accomplished--CEOs, scientists, etc., while the husbands are Neanderthal nerds. Nicole Kidman's Joanna Eberhart was a top TV exec while her husband Walter Kresby (played sheepishly by Matthew Broderick--note that Joanna did not take his last name) is a kind of ordinary joe and caddy to his wife.But what about the premise? Let's be honest guys. Wouldn't it be wonderful if our wives were totally devoted to our comfort and pleasure? If they obeyed us and always kept the house and themselves spotless and catered to our every whim? If they didn't get moody and were always rational, etc.? Isn't it the natural state of the human male to be king of his castle and to have dominion over all he surveys? Well, at any rate here's an update on the Stepford Wives for the new millennium, an expansion of the concept, if you will. Every man has not only a totally devoted and loving wife, but a totally devoted and loving mistress (or two). They would be robots of course, manufactured as consumer goods by General Electric, Microsoft and Intel. But here is the zinger: every woman would have a slavishly devoted husband and secret lover, etc. And while we are at it, they would have loving, appreciative and accomplished robotic children. In other words, nobody would actually interact with other living, breathing human beings. How crude, how disgusting. Instead all humans would interact only with perfect robots perfectly designed to make their world a heaven on earth.Or, more likely we would just be hooked up to an elaborate software program with an utterly convincing virtual reality in which we would have a loyal and loving spouse, two extracurricular lovers..., etc.Fly in the ointment? I think it would be called boredom.Bottom line on this film: a few laughs, a good cast wasted, but a nice documentation of filmland executive stupidity.(Note: Over 500 of my movie reviews are now available in my book "Cut to the Chaise Lounge or I Can't Believe I Swallowed the Remote!" Get it at Amazon!)</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Dismal.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This remake stands as evidence of the consequences of tinkering with a movie's plot when filming has already begun. Wholesale changes were made, apparently, and the resultant film is something of a mess. Despite this, it shows occasional flashes of the bright satire it might have been.The film opens brightly with a neat credits sequence and an amusing riff on reality TV as hard-hearted TV executive Joanna Eberhart (Nicole Kidman, looking very plain and unglamorous throughout) unveils her station's programmes for the new season. Sacked by her network when a contestant from one of her shows kills his ex-wife as a result of being on the show, Eberhart moves with her husband (Matthew Broderick) to the wealthy town of Stepford, where she slowly discovers that things are not as they should be.Nearly all the laughs in this film are contained in the first fifteen minutes (apart from a great AOL dig near the end), but things become increasingly silly once the action moves to Stepford where all the hot wives wear plastic smiles and occasionally move in perfect synchronicity. It's the lazy male's fantasy made real, and there should be a rich vein of humour for writer Paul Rudnick to mine, but unfortunately his script is flat and uninteresting, and modern day sensibilities simply insists that one of the eventual wives is a gay man (Roger Bannister). Once Stepford's wicked secret starts coming to light, the script plays like a 'serious' horror film without all the horror bits taken out as it abandons its increasingly woeful attempts at humour for an ill-conceived mystery that completely fails to involve the viewer.For a smart, hotshot executive, Eberhart is incredibly slow to fathom out what is going on ? in fact she's so slow she has to be told ? and the promotional film of the town's leader, Mike (Christopher Walken), while an amusing riff on 50s promo films, actually flies in the face of all we have seen before. The Stepford wives aren't robots at all ? even though they double as mobile ATM machines ? but real women who have had chips inserted in their heads after "first, we locate the brain." Ah well, the film has lost all audience goodwill by then, anyway, so it doesn't really matter.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Martin Kelly</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Very slight</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>To say that there's not a lot to this film is a bit of an understatement. The filmmakers have taken Ira Levin's fairly unsettling original novel and made a conscious decision to make a pretty light-hearted, comedic version. There's nothing wrong with this in theory but the problem is that they seem to have overstepped the mark on this score and have delivered a movie so insubstantial and lacking in tension/darkness that it's highly unlikely to linger at all in your mind after seeing it and little wonder that audiences and critics were severely underwhelmed at the time of the film's release. The starry cast all perform well enough with Roger Bart in particular delivering an engaging comic turn as one of Stepford's gay couple (a feature which, unsurprisingly, did not feature in the novel or the first film version but which actually works quite well here) but the actors are really taking part in something here which is beneath them. That said, the film is never less than watchable and it certainly looks good throughout. The problem is, it's ultimately a rather silly and forgettable affair which, for a big budget, big studio summer event movie, is disappointing to say the least.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Not the disaster I heard it was</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Powerful TV executive Joanna Eberhart (Nicole Kidman) has a complete nervous breakdown over a tragedy she inadvertently caused. Her husband moves the whole family to the town of Stepford--a quiet little town where Joanna can recover. Joanna notices something strange about the town--all the women LOVE doing housework and cooking, look perfect at all times, are ALWAYS happy and do anything for their husbands. Slowly Joanna realizes something is terribly wrong...and this is a comedy! This remake of the 1975 horror movie takes the plot and (almost) the entire screenplay and turns it into a comedy. The making of this movie (I heard) was a nightmare. Cast member Bette Midler had screaming arguments with director Frank Oz, there were numerous rewrites, there were (at one point) FIVE different endings and other cast members hated the changes made in mid production. Preview audiences hated what the saw and the whole movie was overhauled--many subplots were deleted and the ending was changed! I expected the worst but--it's not too bad.The beginning was dreadful--it was a very unfunny bit about a reality show gone wrong with people being killed. Homicide is not funny. It really picked up when they moved to Stepford. That's when it actually got pretty funny. There were plenty of stupid jokes but the good ones more than made up for them--the introduction of Robo Rover 3000 was VERY funny. It does fall apart at the end but it was still pretty good.The acting--Kidman is just great--she handles the comedy and drama very well; Matthew Broderick (all grown up) was OK as her husband; Bette Midler was hysterical as a very crude neighbor and Glenn Close was just wonderful as the VERY sweet wife of the mayor. Christopher Walken however was DREADFUL as the mayor--but there may have been a reason for that.Not bad but no great shakes. The major problem is the obvious cutting and a bad ending. The infighting couldn't have helped either. Worth seeing--but don't go out of your way.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>To Silly to Be Dark, To Dark To Be Silly</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>For the record, I never saw the original.Plot: .6/2: Joanna Eberhart (Kidman) has had a nervous breakdown. She was a major TV executive until one of the participants on her show (White) gets screwed over and loses his wife in the game and tries to kill both his wife and Joanna. This gets Joanna fired. So her husband Walter Fresby (Broderick) quits his job and gets his family to move to Stepford, a town in Connecticut, to get away from it all. When they arrive, Joanna realizes this place is nothing normal. The whole town is full of women who are apparently perfect. They all have blond hair, do all the housework and have screaming orgasms. All the men go away to the Men's Club on a regular schedule, where they have a meeting hosted by Mike Wellington (Walken), apparently the town leader. Joanna, along with Jewish novelist Bobbie Markowitz (Midler) and flamboyant homosexual Roger Bannister (Bart), apparently the only other normal wives (or in Roger's case, bitches), sets out to figure out what the secret is behind Stepford's closed doors.If you don't know what is wrong with them, you have been hiding in a hole since the '70s. Since everyone knows what the Stepford wives are, there is no shock factor involved in this movie. They told you in the goddamn trailer what was happening. Since it is a remake, none of the plot is surprising, besides one slight twist that might not be completely what Stepford newbies may expect. The problem is that there are about 50 plot holes all over the place. Are the women robots or have they been programmed. It obviously says they are programmed, but there are many instances where it clearly shows them as robots. The ending makes absolutely no sense whatsoever. In fact, most of the movie is nonsensical. If this were an original, I'd be lenient. This is, however, a remake, so I would expect them to build on the original, not make it crappier.Comedy: .7/2: Any comedy in this movie is just a zinger here or there. Granted, those are some good zingers ("I worked at AOL" "Is that why the women are so slow?"), but it rarely ventures outside of that. There are some easy gay jokes involving Roger, some funny Jewish jokes involving Bobbie and a great cameo by Mike White, but it's not enough, and they could have spent much more time making fun of the Stepfordians while instead they tried run of the mill jokes.Suspense: .3/2: Since it is a remake of a movie that coined an almost Dictionary worthy term, there is little suspense left available, probably the reason they went for the comedy angle. They do squeeze a little bit of suspense here and there, but most of it is just too silly to take seriously.Acting: 1.3/2: Oh, the acting is average. Kidman (Eyes Wide Shut) is OK, Broderick (Ferris Bueller's Day Off) is mediocre, Midler (Get Shorty) is good, Bart (the upcoming The Producers) is stereotypical but good, Close (Air Force One) and Walken (The Rundown) are good, but wasted, Lovitz (SNL) is a great choice, but wasted and Mike White is great in his short role. It could have been worse.Entertainment: .5/2: For all the crappy plot, all the crappy zingers and all the crappy suspense, The Stepford Wives could have been worse. It needed to be a lot better, however. It's too silly to take seriously, to dark to be funny.Total: 44%: As I said above, The Stepford Wives is too silly to be suspenseful and too dark to be silly. It has one of the biggest plot holes in recent memories. It could have been good, but it just didn't try hard enough. It was directed by Yoda. That's pretty funny.</fullReview>

Men have all kinds of fantasies, and I for one have never met a man who is turned on by a woman wearing a granny-type nightgown dress. It seems that Stepford would be brimming with wives dressed as everything from Catholic Schoolgirls to mistresses of S &amp; M. I could understand if it were to keep up a public front, but even in their own homes they are dressed in these goofy outfits. The same went for the remake. What man finds that look appealing? Bette Midler in the Paul Prentiss role was her usual heavy-handed self, playing Bette Midler; Matthew Broderick is about as menacing as a puppy. Christopher Walken is creepy in the Patrick O'Neal role, but never with the same subtle and sexy menace. Singer Faith Hill, in a bit part, is very effective as a Stepford Wife. Not that it required any effort on her part, about as much effort as it took Ahhhnold to portray an android in "The Terminator".I kept waiting for Nicole Kidman's forehead to show one iota of expression, but alas she appears to be living happily in the land of regular Botox injections. She seemed determined to try to compensate for her voluntary, expensive paralysis by widening her eyes constantly; a frightening apparition, since her forehead never flinched in tune with the rest of her face. Whatever happened to the Nicole Kidman of such films as "To Die For"? WARNING: SPOILER FOLLOWS! And instead of the women being replaced by robots, the women had computer chips in them. This lead me to wonder how one woman, who was briefly used as an ATM, functioned in that capacity unless she were hollowed out and replaced with machinery first.I'm glad I slept through the last few minutes.</fullReview>

		<username>ShadeGrenade</username>
		<score>0.1</score>
		<shortDescription>Easily The Worst And Most Unnecessary Remake Ever!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Its disturbing to note the number of classic '70's films that have had their reputations trashed by 'remakes' - 'The Wicker Man', 'The Texas Chainsaw Massacre', 'The Omen', 'Assault On Precinct 13' and now this. Ira Levin's novel was not a laugh-out-loud comedy, and neither was Bryan Forbes' 1975 movie, but this Frank Oz-directed film tries to be - and fails dismally.The screenplay adheres closely to the original story, but over-eggs the pudding with 'funny' one-liners, and a ludicrous 'happy' ending which makes the finale of the 1980 'Revenge Of The Stepford Wives' T.V. movie seem believable by comparison.The story was intended as a darkly humorous comment on the women's lib movement, as the male population of a small American town tries to reverse the tide of feminism by murdering their wives and replacing them with robot doubles, replicas both obedient and beautiful. But the tone of the original has been allowed to leak away; this lumbering travesty has the look and feel of a 1960's sitcom. About the only thing missing is a laugh track.As for the performances, Nicole Kidman is so wooden that when she first appeared I thought she had already been 'replaced'. Bette Midler, is as always, terrible. Glenn Close, Christopher Walken and Matthew Broderick could have phoned in their performances.The exact nature of the duplications is confused - are the women being replaced by robots, or simply brainwashed? For most of the picture, it appears to be the former, but when Broderick shuts down the control centre at the climax, the wives regain their personalities, suggesting the latter. What a cop-out.I have heard complaints that the 1975 movie was 'cheesy'. Well, let me put the record straight. Fashions, hairstyles, technology etc. may have changed dramatically since the 70's, but the fact remains that Bryan Forbes' movie is a far better picture than this one. William Goldman's script did not insult the intelligence of the audience, and Katherine Ross and Paula Prentiss outclass Kidman and Midler. On the cheeseometer, the remake registers as pure Stilton.I wonder which sci-fi classic will be next in line for the same treatment. John Frankenheimer's 'Seconds'? Will the remake end with 'Tony Wilson' ( the Rock Hudson character in the original ) cracking lame gags as a Company doctor drills his skull?</fullReview>
	</review>

At this point the story ends. No explanation, you may say.Are you interested in such kind of a story? Well, read the book, since nothing of it the movie offers. In the movie everything is clear: there is somebody changing the women into robots just to make men lazing their lives comfortably. The laboratory is shown, men has remote controls in their pockets. Even more, the reason is told: every one of these women has been once a successful director, manager, a.s.o. And... finally everything is explained plainly, and all the men meet in the shopping center forced to do the shopping.Oh my god, how flat story has been made from a three dimensional book full of interesting questions!</fullReview>

		<username>Roland E. Zwick (magneteach@aol.com)</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>disastrous remake</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>For its 21 Century movie incarnation, Ira Levin's classic feminist thriller "The Stepford Wives" has been re-imagined as a feel-good, slapstick comedy - a shift in tone that is, to put it mildly, a complete disaster for this story. Bryan Forbes' wonderful 1975 movie version (starring Katherine Ross) included many comic scenes as well, but there the humor was pointed and dark, and provided a perfect counterpoint to the sinister thriller elements at the story's core. In this "new and improved," ever-so-sunny edition of the Levin work, written by Paul Rudnick and directed by Frank Oz, the whole thing is played for laughs from the get-go, instantly robbing the story of its edge and diluting its delicious social commentary about a society in which women have literally become mindless robots responding to the commands of their misogynist husbands. All we're left with, in the end, is a really bad, over-the-top comedy that wastes the talents of Nicole Kidman, Matthew Broderick, Glenn Close, Christopher Walken and Bette Midler and that tarnishes the memory of a good movie into the bargain.A pixie-haired Kidman plays Joanna Eberhart, a driven TV network executive who is fired when one of the man-hating programs she produces goes terribly awry. Determined to become a more supportive wife and engaged mother, she allows her husband to cart the family off to a gated, crime-free, all-white, politically conservative (though, somehow, a gay couple is allowed to slip through) community in Connecticut known as Stepford, a 1950's throwback where all the wives are perfect little Susy Homemakers who dress in poofy, pastel-colored gingham dresses, maintain spotlessly clean and flawlessly decorated homes, smile constantly, and dutifully cater to each and every one of their husbands' whims and wishes. Even though Joanna intends to make every effort to become the ideal wife that her understanding husband wants her to be, she soon begins to suspect that there may be a dark secret lurking beneath the impossibly perky dispositions of all the women in town.Although the plot here is virtually the same as it was in the 1975 film, this "Stepford Wives" has taken the entirely wrong approach to the Levin material. Part of what made the earlier version so much fun was that we learned right along with Ross what was actually taking place in the town. Her slow awakening to the truth was what provided the suspense so essential to making the tale frightening and compelling. Here, however, the women are such ridiculous space cadets from the outset that any newcomer would have to be a complete idiot not to figure it all out the moment she hit town. Moreover, as a comedy, the film falls miserably flat, going for the cheap and easy laugh at every opportunity. And, of course, the movie lacks the courage to carry through with the earlier film's downbeat, defeatist ending, opting instead for an upbeat and "clever" surprise-twist finale that shows just how far film-making has fallen over the past 30 years. (It also makes absolutely no sense in light of the scene where Midler puts her hand directly into the flame of a gas stove and suffers no burn - but then who expects coherence and consistency from a film this badly written and thought out?). The irony is that the first version, while retaining the thriller elements of the story, was far more humorous and far more effective as a satire than this limp little remake could ever possibly hope to be.Perhaps, after this movie and "Bewitched," Ms. Kidman should avoid appearing in any more comedies - or at least any more comedies based on decades-old cultural artifacts - for the foreseeable future.This dreadful, ill-fated version of "The Stepford Wives" leaves us with just one basic question: if you're not going to do a remake properly, why do it at all?</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>I won't add to the growing chorus of 'boos' garnered by this godawful remake, except to say that I hate movies based on faulty logic.Without sounding like an economics professor: would the husbands of Stepford really want their wives to abandon their high-paying jobs to become domestic slaves/robots/micro-chip-implanted domestic divas? In a pivotal scene, Nicole Kidman's character discovers that one of the local zombified Stepford women used to be a prominent judge. Bette Midler's character, meanwhile, is supposed to be a best-selling author. Judges and best-seller authors generally make decent salaries.Now, unless the men of Stepford are all multi-millionaires, would they really be willing to so readily sacrifice their wives' earning potential, just so they could enjoy the comforts of robotic domestic divas? More to the point, could anyone besides Donald Trump actually afford to live in Stepford if they didn't come from a high-earning double-income family? In the 1950s era (which this film is allegedly sending up) it was possible to get by with The Man as sole breadwinner, but methinks such days are long gone, except in ultra-rich communities.Just one of the many, many, many flaws in this terrible turkey.Nate H. 		<date>2004-06-16</date>
		<username>guydisney</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A SOLID comedy inspired from a FIRST rate Suspense thriller</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>First and foremost, if you have seen the original film, DO NOT go to this movie expecting to see a "remake" of a classic. Many people argue and deride the current trend of remaking original films that work fine as they are. However if a director quite honestly thinks he can bring something NEW to the original idea and perhaps update the work and bring it to a new audience (i.e. younger viewers) I say HAVE AT IT! It is in THIS spirit that I think Frank Oz successfully achieved his goal. This is a WONDERFUL Comedy that functions ON ITS OWN, even if you have NOT seen the original movie. In case you were unaware (as some of my co-workers were) The original movie debuted in 1975 based on a book by Ira Levin (the author of Rosemary's Baby) The movie was a chilling look at men's and womens role's with a wonderful twist on the suspense horror genre in that the majority of the movie takes place in bright sunshine. The biggest argument I have read in critiques of this current 2004 version of the film, is when it is compared with its' predecessor. This is a flawed argument however. Frank Oz was not ATTEMPTING to do a faithful remake of the original. To be quite blunt without giving spoilers of plot detail, the original movie WOULD NOT WORK as a horror/suspense movie today; its plot and subtext are not only cliché at this point, but too many people in society HAVE seen the original and all twist and reveals of the original would already be familiar to them. It would be like someone remaking "The Sixth Sense" several years from now. This would be largely ineffective since a LARGE portion of the theater going public HAVE seen this film and Do know its basic plot twist.Instead, Frank Oz brings to us a NEW Stepford, which is not only campy by todays societal standards, but Capitalizes on said campiness by becoming in itself a parody of men's and women's roles. The movie has been updated for the 2000s by including a gay couple, and some other small devices. Yes the basic plot is there, but the true gem is the writing. This movie has some WONDERFUL dialogue. I saw the film in a full theater and to be honest I missed several lines because the audience was laughing so hard!The costumes and sets where sumptuous, with GREAT attention to detail. There is humor and foreshadowing all over the place in subtle places. Pay attention to the Symbol of the Mens club on the sign. Look at the roadsigns facing camera as the couple is driving into Stepford. This movie plays VERY well to a family audience. It is rated PG13 which I though appropriate. About the ONLY thing which I thought parents would find objectionable to younger children was a small scene in which two people are heard having sex off-camera. In summary, this is a VERY fine Dark Comedy. I don't think its quite a black comedy like say, "War of the Roses" but more of a campy comedy like "Death Becomes Her". If you go to see a New spin (with new twists) of an original classic, and you go with an open mind, I think you will find a wonderfully fun (if not too serious) motion picture.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>GREAT COMEDY- just don't' take it seriously POSSIBLE SPOILERS</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Too many people reviewing this take the film too seriously- or they went in expecting far too much. I loved the film, however in saying that, it had potential to be a whole lot better.Nicole Kidman's role is pretty bland though, and there's not much she could do with it. Bette Midler is the standout in the film- back in top shape as far as comedy is concerned. Roger Bart is a real discovery in this film- and hopefully he'll be in more comedies in the future. It's nice to see a stereotype in a film after so much political correctness.Having also seen a test screening of the film though, I can reveal Frank oz's original cut was a hell of a lot better than the final cut. There's a thread on the IMDb messageboards were I go into detail on the changes that have been made. The comedy is very Will And Grace, current-events sort of sitcom comedy-a rarity on the big screen. There are some really great jokes (things you can do with a pinecone), parts that amuse (the opening sequence and the reality show satire), and some interesting special effects. However, the new 'Larry King' ending confuses people a lot- as well as the Cartoon bit. These were added after the test screenings, and shouldn't have been. The original ending had the women inside robotic bodies, but there human brains intact. The ending scene has Joanna leaving Stepford, and Bobbie malfunctioning (the 'Goodbye, seeya' bit in the trailer)- and means that there aren't any plot holes in the film.The editing leaves some slow parts in the film, and the movie should have been longer. Frank Oz left out a scene (that will be on the DVD almost definitely), that he'll regret leaving out- and would've livened up the movie so much. In it, Joanna stabs Bobbie (a bit of tension for those who complained the movie wasn't dark enough), she malfunctions and displays her functions- including built in fridge, speakers (head flies off and spins around to demonstrate orgasm), whisk, and lawnmower. All in all though, I thought the movie was great. It had some biting satire, hilarious jokes. I think people are too used to either incredibly witty banter in movies (sort of a cross between Neil Simon, Quentin Tarantino and Jerry Seinfeld) or incredibly stupid gross-out movies- and that's why they couldn't accept this movie.See the Stepford Wives for a good cinema-going experience.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>That was a rhetorical question, by the way. What I mean to say is, what does a version of 'The Stepford Wives' in 2004 have to do with *anything*? A blistering critique of gender roles that haven't existed in American &amp; European society for half-a-century? You can make a film critical of past social mores, but you can't present it as an edgy contemporary satire. It's an "answer film" that's 50 years too late. Imagine if Ted Post had answered John Wayne's 'The Green Berets' with 'Go Tell the Spartans', only in 1997. Maybe the producers can follow up 'The Stepford Wives' with a withering satire of class-snobbery in Victorian England or the hypocrisy of New Left hippies in 1960's America. Because these films would be PRECISELY as relevant and edgy as 'The Stepford Wives'.If you want to see a film that is a great satirical comedy *and* has something to say about gender &amp; feminism in contemporary society, watch 'Fight Club'. Not this drek. Two stars out of ten.~Kurt</fullReview>

		<username>Robert J. Maxwell (rmax304823@yahoo.com)</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Less funny than the original.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is an updated and revised version of the 1975 original. The first version was presented to us as a serious story about the subjugation of women but, like "Mommie Dearest", turned out to be more amusing than dramatic.In this version the comedy inherent in the conceit is out in the open. The only problem is that it's not too funny. Not the fault of the performers, who are all good. Nicole Kidman is a fine actress and comedy is well within her range. Bette Midler, by contrast, is always comic and always Bette Midler. Midler's post-conversion sweetness is hilarious mainly because we see Bette Milder trying so HARD to play "sweet." Mathew Broderick is not bad either, perhaps a bit bland but it fits the part. Glen Close, trapped in her robotic role, is almost unrecognizable beneath the makeup. Christopher Walken gives the best performance of the bunch, as he so often does. Every line he utters has some sort of quirky quality. And he must have enormous self control because he gets the funniest lines and never cracks a smile. Watch him present the TV commercial that will be used "when we go global." He wears a white lab coat, like a TV doctor, and walks the viewer through the robotization process with careless ease. He's even funny after he gets his head knocked off and the sparks fly out.I said it was updated, and what I meant is that there is now a gay couple living in Stepford. And Paula Prentiss has been replaced by Bette Midler, so a lot of jokes about Jews show up -- "why not decorate your menorah with pine cones?" "How about if I just arrange a lot of pine cones on my front lawn so that they spell out BIG JEW?" The original had to do with men feeling threatened by their wives' desire to raise their consciousness, whatever that means, and to develop interests outside the home. This version has gone beyond that vague feeling of oppression. The Stepford Wives are now all ex-CEOs, brain surgeons, and Pulitzer Prize winners. Even the robot terrier was a prize winner. The men no longer feel threatened. They stopped worrying about that years ago. Now they just feel humiliated and beaten by their more successful spouses.What humor there is can be found mostly in one-liners. Wisecracks that are sometimes funny imposed on an essentially humdrum script. The situations themselves aren't amusing. There is, for instance, no scene to equal the one in which Katherine Ross stabs the now-robotic Paula Prentiss in the vicinity of her belly button, shorts out a couple of anodes, and sends Prentiss into a stereotyped infinite loop of behavior -- going through the motions of serving coffee but letting the cups drop on the floor and so on. Now THAT'S funny.</fullReview>

		<username>ctomvelu</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Funny but flawed</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>SPOILER ALERT: This flat-out, broadly played comic remake of a 1970s sci-fi thriller has a terrific cast and some truly funny lines, as a New York City couple (a smart and sassy but repressed, dressed-in-black TV exec Nicole Kidman and her woebegone schmuck of a corporate drone husband Matthew Broderick) moves to woodsy lower-Fairfield County, Connecticut to start over again. Only they find they are surrounded by a bunch of drop-dead gorgeous robot women, wholly subservient to their geeky menfolk and acting and dressing as if it were the '50s all over again. Much merriment ensues. The film is harmed on two counts: It is unclear about to what degree the wives are robots. (In fact, all that remains of the originals are their brains which have been reprogrammed, meaning they are actually androids -- and this point is not properly handled, leaving viewers easily confused.) Plus the flick doesn't know when to stop and has no fewer than three endings. It should have stopped after the first one, which involves a thoroughly transformed Nicole Kidman grocery shopping. Instead, the screenwriters go for a very awkward, politically correct, series of happy endings, and mess up badly. Well, having said that, see it anyhow for Kidman and Bette Midler as her nonconforming, sad-sack writer friend who becomes the Betty Crocker to end all Betty Crockers. Midler's transformation is the closest tie this amusing little flick has to the downbeat but clever, sometimes sinister original. Both are based on a popular satire by the author who brought us ROSEMARY'S BABY: Ira Levin, a Yale grad who definitely knows from certain lower-Fairfield County types. Heck, I've met a few of these robot types myself, in places where this film was shot, including Darien and Greenwich. Where better to hide robots, indeed?</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Andy (film-critic)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>"Where would no one notice a town full of robots?" Connecticut. (SPOILERS)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What a sad, sad film. The Stepford Wives only proves that big stars and a big name director like Frank Oz do not always make good pictures. There was nothing worth saving from this movie. From the opening scenes to the final credits, and all that I found lying in-between was pure gibberish. It is annoying to find a film with such decent actors to be so horribly bad. Kidman has been working very hard lately to make a name for herself in the Hollywood community that a film like this could debunk her. From the story, to the poorly mismatched characters, to the cheap CGI, this film smelled of recycled garbage from the opening. It should have been returned, but instead I forged ahead and watched.As bad as this film was, there was a glimmer of light at the beginning. Kidman's character is somewhat humorous as the television executive with ideas on how to make women more powerful on screen. What she shows her shareholders are tiny morsels of comedy, but those are quickly eaten nothing is left behind except for crumbs. From the moment that she leaves the podium, her role is over. Kidman tries to capture the essence of her character, but just cannot. I have trouble-seeing Kidman as a comic actress, especially when she has mastered the drama/thriller/musical genre so well. The lack of comfortability with her character is ultimately what flaws Kidman's performance from the start. You just never see her excitement or comfort level in her eyes. You never saw her true character. This goes the same with everyone else in this film. Broderick was the worst decision as her husband. They had no chemistry, no love, and no passion. I saw them as two actors with lines instead of a secure relationship. Couple this with more horrible performances by Midler (who never quite reaches the high of her character), Glenn Close (who seemed like she was over-the-top with her character and just finally happy to be in a film with some big name stars), Lovitz (what was he doing in this movie?), and even the always lovable Walken (who seemed stale and sterile throughout the entire film) just flopped. The actors and their inability to carry this film to a higher level was only half the issue with this film, the other problem lied in the hands of director Frank Oz.What was he thinking in this film? It felt like his children got into the editing room and spilled glue on the cut film and that was the way he edited it. There was no continuity at all. We would jump from one location to the next and then further again without any warning or care. This creates a sense of distraction and unease in the tone of the film. Secondly, did Oz know what this film was about? At one point I thought that these women were robots, and the next they are humans with computer chips in their minds. What about the sparks? What about the ATM? This never seemed to fit at all. It was as if Oz had made two films and spliced the beginning of one and the end of the other together. First time filmmakers could have done a better job than Frank Oz on this film. I needed this to be a very dark comedy (if even a comedy at all). Where was the darkness in this film? I will tell you ? THERE WASN'T ANY! If there was anything at all in this film, I would give credit, but there has been nothing redeemable about this film at all. The choppy direction that Oz perfects jumbles the themes and tones to nearly nothing. I could not honestly sit here and tell you what the tone of the film was. I think it was locked somewhere in Oz's mind, and it couldn't get out. This goes the same with the theme of the film. Does it have something to do with perfection or feminism? A happy home or being wealthy? Maybe it has to do with cooking cup cakes over pies? How about playing God in suburbia? Who knows? I couldn't tell, and I am definitely not willing to watch it again to find out. Overall, this was the worst film that I have seen this year by far. The ending was laughable and just plain stupid, as you could probably say the same for the rest of the film. All the actors did the worst jobs they could and it became increasingly obvious that Oz should just go back to doing Muppet voices. There is nothing of value in this movie and if you chose to spend the money to see it than at least you have been warned. This is yet another perfect example of a Hollywood blunder. Where was the Inspector Gadget music when you needed it? BLAH!Grade: * out of *****</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Stepford-Far From Perfect</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Stepford Wives is a movie that reeks of being in a hurry. Even though the filming schedule was elongated, the final product doesn't show it. The sets look as if they came under budget on time and effort. They look cheap and quickly thrown together. The change from Manhattan city life to perfect Stepford land is unnoticeable in the way the dress, setting and style of the film and characters appear. Because of this, it's clear that the ?clean and neat' style the film may have been going for is not a commentary on its theme of perfection, but simply the lack of effort to make it otherwise. The story is adapted from the original thriller, The Stepford Wives and hence the originality of its plot is not something the crew of this production can take credit for. Joanna Eberhart (Nicole Kidman) has lost her position has a high ranking official in the television world and, with her husband (Matthew Broderick) and their children, moves to Stepford, the setting of this mediocre thriller and `mystery'. Why are these women so perfect? Where do the men go all day? What do they do there? The audience knows the answers and so we watch Kidman try to answer these questions but instead simply establish herself as a drama queen. Her performance lacks originality and, although she is believable, she has not completely separated herself from Nicole into Joanna. The supporting cast is comic relief (and they're not really all that comical). Bett Middler plays the odd girl and with her gay sidekick, they join Joanna in an attempt to answer their questions about the robotic barbies posing as wives, but instead feel like extra baggage. Of course, it's just now that Joanna's there that the preoccupied feminist and her Queer Eye clone have decided that there's something strange going on. That's the movies for you. Or rather, that's Stepford. The cast and crew of this movie knew it was bad. They refused comment on it. They went back and attempted re-shoots to improve open it's flaws. But however it happened, a week of pick-up shots can't change the problem of this movie. In fact, the entire thing looks like it was filmed with ten different directors over the course of ten months. Every aspect of each scene looks like it was stolen from twenty different movies and then photoshopped to fit the current directors image. 		<username>BradBate</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Miss Piggy had more bite, and generally speaking, better lines.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This may be one of those movies that no one likes but the people; the public screening I attended had the audience in stitches, including my 80-year-old mother-in-law, who pronounced it, 'cute.' But the Frank Oz remake of, 'The Stepford Wives,' may also bomb at the box office, not only of its own failings (and there are many), but of the negative publicity the production has received for months. In the re-imagining of the creepy 1975 original, Joanna Eberhart (Nicole Kidman) comes to Stepford, Connecticut with her husband (Matthew Broderick) and children, only to find a community of wives, led by real estate agent Claire Wellington (Glenn Close), who seem to be Barbie Doll realizations of 1950's-style female servitude. They are picture-perfect, sexually voracious, robotic re-creations of the wives of Stepford men. The first film, from a script by the legendary screenwriter William Goldman, targeted devious husbands; the new film, written by Paul Rudnick, satirizes overachieving, workaholic wives. The first film sought to be a psychological thriller; the re-make seeks to be a comedy. And in all fairness, there are some funny scenes and some good lines. But overall, the film falls short on comedic bite, and leaves most of the best one-liners to secondary characters. Bette Midler is amusing as a rebellious, Betty Friedan-esque writer (Bobby Markowitz), who is as amazed as Joanna by the behavior of the dollbots. They team up with another Stepford newcomer, Roger Bannister (Roger Bart), a very catty, 'girlfriend,' whose gay lover has found acceptance in the diabolical Stepford Men's Club, headed by Wellington's husband Mike (Christopher Walken). Together, they're going to get to the bottom of things. Kidman and Walken are said to have both had problems with the script, Walken reportedly engaging in shouting matches with Oz in the director's office. Ego-charged bickering on the set, coupled with the director's perfectionism, apparently put the project behind schedule. Kidman has said she took the role when Producer Scott Rudin convinced her it would be a very light workout in comparison to her Oscar-winning turn in 'The Hours,' which had required that she work about three weeks. 'Scott said to me, 'Do this movie, and it will feel like summer camp,' Kidman told the Ladies Home Journal. Instead, it turned into a marathon she had not experienced since Stanley Kubrick's, 'Eyes Wide Shut.' When 'Stepford' production went over schedule, Kidman had to drop out of the thriller, 'Mr. &amp; Mrs. Smith,' with Brad Pitt. When 'Stepford' finally wrapped, she went to work on Sidney Pollock's, 'The Interpreter,' then had all sorts of scheduling problems when Oz needed her back for re-shoots. As production dragged on, Midler found herself splitting her time between the film's shooting demands and the necessity of rehearsing for her huge 'Kiss My Brass' concert tour that had long been scheduled. Director Frank Oz has made about seven comedies since his days as Jim Henson's strong right arm at the Muppets factory, and one pretty taut thriller. His 'Dirty Rotten Scoundrels,' with Steven Martin, Michael Caine and Glenne Headly, is a comedy classic, and the Rudnick-penned, 'In and Out,' with Kevin Klein, was wonderful. Recognized for providing the classic voice for Yoda in the 'Star Wars' films, and for his performances for years as Miss Piggy, Fonzie Bear, Bert, Grover, and the Cookie Monster, all from the Muppets menagerie, Oz is known as a genuinely nice person. But this is his second round of difficulties with actors. In his excellent thriller, 'The Score,' notoriously difficult Marlon Brando refused to take direction from Oz, forcing the British-born director to use co-star Robert DeNiro as an intermediary.Is 'The Stepford Wives' worth seeing? Maybe, but it will require some effort on your part. Here's my suggestion. Put together a group of your brightest, funniest, most outgoing friends and go to the movie. As the picture starts drawing to a close (the beginning of the ballroom scene will be your cue), leave the theater, repair to the nearest venue for adult beverages, and write your own ending. Even your dullest pals will come up with something better than what the nervous Paramount executives forced on Oz and his dispirited cast.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>When I saw 'The Stepford Wives' the theater workers accidentally but in the reel of the third 'Harry Potter' film, causing us to wait about twenty minutes more to see the movie. They made a mistake. So did Frank Oz in making this film.'The Stepford Wives' is the twelfth 2004 movie I've seen. The others consisted of masterworks ('Kill Bill: Vol. 2', 'The Passion of the Christ' and 'Spartan'), fun, shallow stuff ('Troy', 'Miracle', 'Shrek 2', 'Secret Window' and 'Hellboy'), and stupidly funny romps ('50 First Dates' and 'Dodgeball'). The last movie was 'You Got Served' ? the only one of all eleven that was actually worse than 'Stepford'.The story focuses on Joanna (Nicole Kidman) and Walter (Matthew Broderick), husband and wife who move to Stepford, a town where everything seems perfect. But, as we all know, if there is something perfect in a movie, there is something horrifying and, well, imperfect behind it. In this film, the men control their women with remote controls. Joanna, with the help of an angry writer (Bette Midler) who is ? ha ha! ? Jewish, and a flamboyant gay (Roger Bart), investigates what's up.Spoilers, I guess.I think it's common knowledge by now that the women all of microchips in their brains. I don't even want to list all the flaws that this plot point brings up, but I will anyway. 1) The men are frequently calling the women robots ? even though they're apparently flesh-and-blood humans. 2) At one point a woman takes a credit card in her mouth and spits out bills ? even though she's just human. 3) In another scene a woman's breasts enlarge with the push of a button?I didn't know they could do that! 4) A character is found out to be a robot, even though no character had ever implied that they had any knowledge of making robots. And, finally, the big one: 5) just moments after finding out that the women have chips in their brains (and are NOT robots) Joanna sees a robot ready to replace her.Now, I know, it's not supposed to be an accurate movie and you're not supposed to pin point all the flaws, but these are some huge, glaring flaws.They're the Jabba the Hut of plot flaws. Honestly, what the hell were the screenwriters thinking? Did they think the audience wouldn't notice? Did they notice? Were the test screenings held in a kindergarten class? No, they mustn't have been, kindergarteners would notice these errors.But, just because my review is negative I'm not going to lie, it delivered some laughs (but just a few). Mostly in the first hour, they fade away once the movie starts going for horror and (bad) melodrama. As for acting, well, Kidman and Broderick do what they can, and Midler seems to be having fun, but they can't save it. The only performance I really liked was Christopher Walken, and that's only because he's Christopher Walken and can make any movie a teensy-weensy bit better (though I have yet to see 'Gigli').The film has two climaxes, the first of which there is no buildup to, the second is just too long and, well, we don't care. The climaxes describe the film ? uneven, unexciting, unfunny, unsurprising. 5/10.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>This is a remake of the 1975 film but the emphasis here is on comedy and not horror which only makes sense because the so-called secret is shown in all the trailers for the film. Story is about Joanna Eberhard (Nicole Kidman) who is fresh from being fired as a television executive and going through a mental breakdown so her husband Walter Kresby (Matthew Broderick) moves her and their two kids to Stepford, Conn. They move in and discover that the wives of their community are unusually cheerful and domesticated. They are also very beautiful and spend their days cleaning house and baking and engaging in sex during the afternoon. This irks Joanna but she meets author Bobbie Markowitz (Bette Midler) who has also just moved into the community with her family and together they discuss how different everyone is compared to them.*****SPOILER ALERT*****Walter enjoys his new home and community and he regularly goes to the local Men's Club which is run by Mike Wellington (Christopher Walken) and soon enough all the husbands show Walter what the secret is in Stepford. Everyone's wife has had computer chips installed in their brain and the husbands have a remote control that can make them do anything they want. Walters marriage is in trouble and he loves his new life but now he must decide if he should go along with the others or not.This film is directed by veteran Frank Oz who is a very good director but reportedly this script went through a few re-writes and re-shoots and the end result is a pretty lightweight and politically correct film. The one aspect of the story from the original 1975 film still remains interesting today and that's the angle about men and do they still want their wives to be homemakers. It's still a pertinent issue especially in todays times where marriages usually have the husband and wife both working. But aside from that and the sometimes funny lines that are given this is a film that ends up being predictable with an overly simplistic ending. I do have one question though and it involves that replica of Kidman laying on the table. What is it's purpose? If the secret is that computer chips are being installed in the wives brains then I don't understand the use of a replica. But, I guess that's nit picking but this is a film that seems below the talent of it's actors. The 1975 version is a dark horror film that had audiences talking about the underlying message but this updated story has it's emphasis on comedy and when it's over there is nothing to discuss about it. It makes no impact whatsoever.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-06-25</date>
		<username>movieguy1021 (Movieguy1021@comcast.net)</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>The Stepford Wives: 3/10</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Usually I have no problems with remakes if they're done successfully. Of course, that didn't authorize Gus Van Sant's Psycho, but that's beside the point. Frank Oz (who many recognize as the voice of Yoda and Miss Piggy from the Muppets, among others), already had one successful remake under his belt (the enjoyable Little Shop of Horrors), but needed more. So he decided to remake an obscure 1975 thriller about a town that's too perfect, The Stepford Wives. And now I think I'm on the bandwagon on saying that all of these remakes need to be stopped NOW.Joanna (Nicole Kidman, badly masking her accent yet again) is a successful TV person (executive? president? I don't remember), but after there's an assassination attempt on her (during a 15 minute overview of the new season-completely unnecessary [except for a 'pun' later]), she's fired. She and her less successful husband Walter (Matthew Broderick, who seems to be underused lately) move with their two kids to Stepford, Connecticut, where it seems like everyone's stuck in the 1950's. But something's not right, as Joanna senses, and tries to discover what it is, while Walter gets dragged into the chauvinistic society that the Stepford husbands share. Random characters (including that ubiquitous Christopher Walken) ensue.I could reiterate what every reviewer has been saying. I could say how this attempt at comedy, drama, and horror tries to be all three and fails at all three-which it does, completely. It falls over on its padded-runtime face many times whenever it tries to be one of the three. When it tries to be funny, it just isn't: you can flush the toilets from a control panel by the front door. Now isn't that hilarious? And when Walter has to choose between Joanna and a "perfect" wife (you'll understand if you see the movie...but you really shouldn't see it), the drama's supposed to be running high up. DUH! What do you think's gonna happen? And the whole concept (which might have been scary in the 1975 version, I didn't see it) just isn't scary. All of these problems don't lie in Oz (it seems like he's doing a bit of struggling for work), but in Paul Rudnick's screenplay. He tries to squeeze so much into it that it just doesn't work. Any of the plot "twists" that come about can be seen basically during the opening credits. Well, not the "final" twist, but that's too stupid to even mention.Characters run to and fro without an iota of development (Jon Lovitz was in the movie?) and pop in just to say "funny" things or to help the "plot" along. But that's not even the worst of it. In every single scene-no exaggeration-there is either a boom mike or part of the camera in frame. I can forgive one or two-those things are pretty hard to avoid-but Stepford is such a slipshod, one-hand-tied-behind-your-back movie that they just don't care about making something that's technically well done. The only thing well done about this movie is Roger Bart as one of Stepford's only two gay men (such a stretch from his role as Carmen Ghia in the Broadway version of The Producers). Without his small character, the movie would be laugh less, every character uninteresting, and, well, the movie would still be awful. Basically, stay away from The Stepford Wives.My rating: 3/10Rated PG-13 for sexual content, thematic material and language.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-02-16</date>
		<username>mentalcritic</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>A strong indication of the times we live in, and not a good one</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In 1975, a film adaptation of an Ira Levin novel called The Stepford Wives created an inescapable portrait of a realm in which mind control was no longer a painstaking science but simply replacing the original subject with a robotic clone. It is still frightening today, not just to women who might have things in common with its protagonist, but to individuals of all shades belonging to marginalised groups whom individuals with a less than noble agenda attempt to silence or put words into the mouths of. Which is partly what makes this lemon of a remake so insulting. Exactly where Frank Oz got the idea that mind control and identity erasure is a fit subject for comedy from, I doubt we will ever know, but he clearly did not pay nearly as much attention as he should have during his association with Jim Henson. However, a lot of warning signs that occurred before the film was completed may in fact suggest that Oz may not deserve all of the blame for this atrocity. For one thing, expecting a film to handle an adult subject like this in an adult manner is rather optimistic at the best of times.For another, the constant-rewrite culture of present-day Hollywood has ensured that the vision of the creative team behind the film will be subverted in favour of the whims of any ignorant fool in the audience. One scene in the men's club shown in this film practically announces that this is exactly what happened here. Other scenes pertaining to exactly what has been done to the women of Stepford in order to make them so malleable allude to a mind control chip that can be implanted in the brain and shorted out or otherwise made defective, much like a RAM chip. But this particular scene has one man swiping a card of some sort across his wife's head, and a bill being spat from her mouth. Apparently, audiences wanted an overtly happy ending, and Paramount did not give a damn about how logical the one they grafted onto the end of the film would appear. It isn't just the wives in this instance, Paramount literally wanted a Stepford audience.Any film with Bette Midler in it is going to be the subject of derision for her inability to play anyone other than herself, but she has never revealed exactly how non-divine she is quite like she has in The Stepford Wives. Her performance itself is nothing remarkable, much like the rest of her acting career. Rather, it is her talking to the press that really messed her up. Probably the most widely-circulated quote from her regarding The Stepford Wives is to the effect that this is what all men want from the women they associate with. Part of the horror that the original captured so well derives from the fact that real men would find such a compliant, obedient automaton repulsive. If you need any proof of this, ask yourself why there is a whole civil rights movement dedicated to having governments recognise the rights of certain neurodiverse individuals, man, woman, and child, to refuse to be given invasive brain surgery simply to make their behaviour more like that of John Typical. Apparently Midler has such a narrow mind that even skin colour does not make people different when they are masculine.Script and editing aside, quite probably the worst of the problems with The Stepford Wives circa 2004 was the support cast. After two of the Cusack siblings decided this project was not worth their time, Hollywood went to every star in the phonebook they could find. The results are somewhat mixed. The casting of Matthew Broderick in the lead male role sets up a great visual joke in which, after delivering a somewhat contrived speech to the superficial-level effect of what I have so far been saying about how automatons rarely make a worthwhile substitute for the real thing, the real-man status of the character is commented upon. It just makes it such a pity that very few actual men were involved either behind or in front of the camera. Christopher Walken apparently refused to finish the film without a happy ending. Midler went hog wild demanding her own modifications to the story. Frank Oz was unable to treat this creative idiocy in the manner it deserved. The result is a film that cannot make up its mind whether it is a comedy, a satire, a horror, or a Sweet Valley High novel. Calling the end result flat would be flattery.For this reason, and many others, I gave The Stepford Wives circa 2004 a two out of ten. The novel and the original adaptation were meant to be future-shock tales of what will happen if we allow the mind control we have begun to accept in our society to go too far. The 2004 version feels like the result of the minds behind the original being lobotomised, if that tells you anything.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A Fun Version of The Stepford Wives but not a Laugh Out Riot</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This version of the Stepford Wives is played out for laughs unlike the novel or Original Stepford Wives movie in 1975.In this version Joanna Eberhart played by Nicole Kidman is a high powered TV mogul rather than a free lance photographer and she was born an Eberhart and kept her maiden name when she married Walter who's last name is Kresby.Eberhart put on one too many Alternative Programs on her network, and it almost gets her killed. However it costs her job, and she falls to pieces. Walter decides to move to Stepford in hopes of starting a new life.Eberhart is not crazy about being a stay at home wife and mother but she jumps into the role with two feet and makes the best out of it. Soon she meets the other women of Stepford and all their perfection.Something to note here Faith Hill plays Stepford wife Sarah Sunderson and is a mixture of Patricia Cornell and Carol Van Sant in this movie.Soon enough Eberhart runs into not only into Bobbie Markowitz (Bette Midler) but also Roger Bannister (Roger Bart) who is Gay and moved into Stepford with his partner. Bart is the feminine one so he bonds with Joanna and Bobbie. Meanwhile at the Men's Assocation you see the men, act more like boys with their toys (no foolin) rather than the workaholic held back men in the 75 version.Because Bart acts like a woman he is the first of the three to be Stepfordtized and he becomes this Social Conservtive type after the change. Soon enough Bobbie gets the Stepford treatment and we see the recreation of the scenes of Joanna's face off at the men's association, but this face it have a feel of a boys club occult ceremony mixed in with a little Frankenstein on the table as Joanna sees her "replacement." You then see the recreation of the Grocery Store scene which wraps up the 75 version but in this version the movie it does not end their.The climax is a ballroom dancing night and Walter sets things in motion that helps the Stepford Wives get the upper hand, but it also reveals a few surprises in which everyone gets the shock of their life, and leads to a commentary that goes beyond 1970's women's lib (which was the basis of The Stepford Wives). Rather a statement is made on how life in America has changed but not all for the better. Some of that is seen early in the film including Eberhart's love for black clothing, which respects the "working woman", and the open titles in which you see film clips of 1950's housewives.If you plan on seeing this film you may want to see the original 75 version first. You might be able to appreciate this version afterwords, and if not you can see how they play and complement (or tries to) each other.CNN's Larry King and Meredith Vieira (The View and Millionaire) have cameos in this movie.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A huge mess</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Word was leaked early that this film was a disaster. Test screenings went miserably and the ending had to be re-filmed. It sat on the shelves for close to a year. Nicole Kidman said nasty things about director Frank Oz behind his back, and the cast and crew in turn said things about her. Matthew Broderick said it was one of his worst filming experiences of his career.And then the movie came out and flopped. It deserved to. It's a huge mess - an uninspired, silly, over-the-top, stupid, insulting, smug, self-conscious little "comedy" that is too nice to be dark and too dark to be nice.The first half is pretty clever and I found it amusing. Nicole Kidman has a nervous breakdown and moves out of the city with her husband (Broderick) to get away from the chaos. Once there they find a home in a small community named Stepford, begun by a local millionaire (Christopher Walken).She makes some friends with neighbors Jon Lovitz and Bette Midler, but soon realizes the local "perfect wives" are indeed just that - and may in fact be robots.The movie tries to be a satire of our repression of women's rights and so on and so forth. It tries to criticize the lustful sexual appetite of men and say that women are more than just bodies. They have souls and feelings and emotions.But this is a simple point and even by today's standards it's outdated itself - this was far more prescient back in the '50s and '60s when the original took place. Nowadays it seems redundant and smug.The ending is where it all falls apart - it was obviously reshot to be "happier" and you can even tell because Nicole Kidman's hair is completely different at the end of the movie, which implies she was brought back months later for the re-shoot.It's a horrible little film that is instantly forgettable and annoying.</fullReview>

		<username>(themoviemark@themoviemark.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Worse than I expected</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I have a confession to make. I wasn't very excited about seeing this movie. Nothing about it really grabbed my interest. But I decided to watch it with an open mind and give it a fair chance to win me over. After seeing it, I must admit that it far exceeded my expectations ... OF CRAPPINESS!!!Unbelievable. This movie does nothing but leave me with several questions: Why was it made? Seriously, what is the point of the movie? Social commentary? Let me see, there's no such thing as a perfect world, right? There, I just summed it up for you and saved you the price of a movie ticket. No need to thank me.What was Nicole Kidman thinking? Did she read the script before she signed on? It's one of the stupidest movies I've ever seen on the big screen, and folks, I've seen a LOT of movies on the big screen. Everything about it is just very, very lame. It's one of those movies where you're embarrassed for everybody on screen, and you're embarrassed to be in the audience. It's hard to know what to say because I'm just baffled by the whole thing.Even more baffling is that a lot of the audience was laughing during the movie. AT WHAT?!?!?! What, pray tell, is so funny about lines such as "I didn't marry something from Radio Shack"? NOTHING! So why was the audience laughing?? What's so funny about a CGI robot dog falling down the stairs? NOTHING! So why did I hear some guy laughing so hard that I thought for sure he was coughing up a whole Auntie Anne soft pretzel? I don't get it.Story wise, this movie is all over the place. Does it want to be a comedy? Well, it fails because I refuse to laugh at Bette Midler wearing fake antlers. I heard it was supposed to be somewhat of a dark comedy, but it's nowhere near edgy enough to qualify. In fact, it's nowhere near the edge of anything. It's resting comfortably in a barcalounger in Topeka, KS. Sorry, but mocking reality television just doesn't cut it anymore.Is it supposed to be an emotional drama? There were some long stretches of Nicole Kidman and Matthew Broderick sharing their feelings, but the only emotion I was feeling was one of sheer sadness because I realized valuable moments of my life were flittering away, never to be seen again. Wow, Kidman loves Broderick because of a dance he does when he wins computer chess. Where's a remote when you need it? Seriously, I had not one iota of interest in a single character.I guess in the case of fairness, I should point out that I don't like Glenn Close, Matthew Broderick, or Bette Midler, and this movie only reminded me why. Glenn Close was absolutely annoying (especially in the totally unfunny exercise scene), and, I'm not *trying* to be mean, but she looked like a witch. I suppose women over the age of 50 might laugh at Close swiveling her hips and singing "chooga chooga chooga," but I failed to crack a smile.Bette Midler (who I initially mistook for Whoopi Goldberg) did nothing but make me pray for her character's imminent death. So she fancies herself a "Jew with an attitude" who writes books about her mother called, "I Love You, But Please Die"? If y'all will excuse me, I've gotta go see a doctor to tend to my busted gut. Let me try to make this perfectly clear. There is one scene where Nicole Kidman is about to catch on that some of the women are robots (which is a major plot hole when compared to the tagged-on-at-the-last-second ending) and makes a comment about how sparks were coming out of her ears. Bette Midler replies, and I'm paraphrasing, "That's a sure sign of bad jewelry." The audience howled with laughter. I sighed, shook my head, and asked God why He was doing this to me. Which reaction do you more relate to? Use that as a guide to decide whether or not to see the movie.And Matthew Broderick? Well, he continued his transformation into Jerry Mathers and offered no sign that he has an ounce of coolness or testosterone in him. So basically, he's been typecast. Even Christopher "I'll Appear in Any Crap I'm Paid For These Days" Walken didn't do anything of note except make you think to yourself, "Kangaroo Jack, Gigli, and now this? Ouch."I've never seen the original movie, and I'm really not moving mountains to clear my schedule to do that, but I heard it was a thriller. It appears this movie makes a stab at providing a few thrills, but it just doesn't work. Is the audience actually supposed to think it's a big mystery as to what's going on in town? Are we actually supposed to be surprised at the "twist" near the end of the movie? I don't know. There isn't anything shocking or surprising about the movie, but it sure feels like we're supposed to think there is.Welp, I've wasted enough of my life dwelling on this. If I had to do it all over again, then I'd choose not to see it. Not even for free. I thought it was equally as bad as (and maybe even worse than) last year's "Legally Blonde 2."If you wanna yell and scream at me for blasting this movie, then go ahead. I'm sure it'll have a fan base. For some reason, Bette Midler has a fan base, so I have no doubt this movie will as well. But it sure doesn't include anybody by the name of Johnny Betts. And if it does, then I'm gonna ask him to change his name.</fullReview>
	</review>
